Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
ii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
This thesis focuses on the use of balloon framing with walls in cross-laminated timber (CLT) as a sta-
bilizing element in medium-rise office buildings. A case study is made on a plan layout, designed by
Waugh-Thistleton Architects, of an office building with wood structures. The building is carried by
columns and beams in glued laminated timber as well as core wall in CLT in the center, which is also
intended to stabilize the building against lateral loads. The plan layout has been investigated for stabi-
lization of the building with 3-8 stories. The layout has been optimized by moving a small number of
doors to adjacent walls and by minor relocation of the room distribution.
It has been analyzed, how the load is distributed to the stabilizing elements using numerical and analytical
approaches. The stiffnesses has shown to play a major role in this regard, and different results can be
obtained by the use of different methods. The tributary area method has yielded to be mostly conservative,
but different methods can be conservative for different scenarios. When using elements of different
stiffness, a load distribution method that takes this into account, results in a more optimal load distribution.
Through analyzing the stabilization of the building, it has been found, that the proposed, optimized plan,
has a sufficient number of walls to stabilize the building for up to 5 stories. For 6-8 stories, additional
or alternative stabilizing elements are needed. By addition of two or four CLT-walls to the plan beyond
the core, the stabilizing system can be sufficient for up to 7 stories. A significant number of CLT-walls
must be added, for the stabilizing system to be sufficient for 8 stories. Lateral deformation, anchoring
and in-plane shear and axial stresses, has shown to be governing factors for the design of the stabilizing
CLT-walls. By adding bracing in wood or steel in the gables, the stabilizing system can be sufficient for at
least up to 8 stories. Alternatively, the core can be constructed of prefabricated concrete elements, where
post-tensioning is necessary for more than 5-6 stories.
The vertical shear connection between two CLT wall-panels has also been investigated experimentally
and analytically. The connection should have sufficient stiffness, for the wall panels to approach the
behavior of a single wall, as this will lower anchor reactions as well as stresses and deformations in the
wall. Furthermore, strength, buildability and ductility has been considered, leading to the conclusion,
that a good solution is to use dowels with two recessed steel plates along the height of the wall. The
experimental analysis yielded high capacity and stiffness, but also uncertainties to the analytical formulas,
as the results deviates from the calculations.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

==PAGE:8==
1
Introduction and Project Description
1.1
Background
Due to global warming, it has become desirable to decrease emissions of greenhouse gasses like carbon
dioxide (CO2). A way to do this is by efficiently using wood as a structural element, as an alternative
to steel and concrete, which are heavy contributors to CO2-emissions in the building industry. More
research and experience are needed on wood-structures, if it shall compete with steel and concrete, as
they are very well-known materials [1] [2].
Building with wood offers other advantages as well. When designed right, buildings can be built fast,
and many elements can be prefabricated, reducing time spent on the building-site. The low weight also
reduces the loads on the foundation, which may lead to a lower material usage in this regard. This also
means, that elements often can be thinner, increasing the utilization of the rooms. These properties also
contribute positively to the environmental aspect. If the wood is not reused at the end of its life, the
(CO2) will normally be re-released into the atmosphere through burning. It is therefore also important
to incorporate re-usage of the wood at this stage of life [2]. Lower tolerances are also allowed when
building with wood, when comparing with e.g., concrete [1]. Other advantages are the lower noise during
the construction phase, and the generally cleaner building-site, when building with wood.
There are, however, many complexities to be solved, such as fire safety, acoustics, structural connections,
and especially stability, before wood can be adapted as a common structural material for taller buildings.
In Europe, wood-based solutions are relatively common for low-rise buildings, but not for taller buildings.
However, more taller buildings in wood have been built through the later years, e.g., MjГёstГҘrnet (2019) in
Norway, Treet (2015) in Norway and Kajstaden Tall Timber Building (2019) in Sweden. Build-In-Wood
[3] is a project funded by EU, that strives towards normalization of wood and wood-based products in
the building industry, and simultaneously making it affordable and in high quality. They divide their
project into 10 so-called work packages, of which no. 2 regards building systems. This work package is
led by Waugh-Thistleton Architects (WTA) [4], which is an architecture firm, that is based in London.
The consortium for the project is international, and Technological Institute of Denmark (DTI) serves as
coordinator to the project.
WTA has designed a floor plan for an office building through collaboration with DTI, which is the case
study for this project. The floor plan consists of a common layout, thereby representing a common office
building. A masterвҖҷs thesis by Dimmock [5] has been done on the same project case for the building
with 5 stories, where anchoring connections of a stabilizing CLT-wall was investigated analytically and
experimentally.
1.2
Objective and Aim
The proposed architectural plan by WTA as well as the design strategy included in the design guide from
Build-in-Wood serves as basis for this project. The aim is to minimize material consumption, and to
maximize the use of mass timber in the stabilizing system of the building, by utilizing the load-bearing
walls on the proposed floor plan. Furthermore, the proposed plan should be as open and flexible as
possible, when investigating the possibility of additional stabilizing elements. Another aim, mainly rel-
evant for connection design, is to maximize buildability, which is considered by availability, installation
complexity and time spent on the building site.
The objective is to investigate the capacity and function of the stabilizing system of the building. This
is done, firstly, by investigating how many stories can be built without additional alternatives, before the
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
1
==PAGE:9==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION AND PROJECT DESCRIPTION
walls on the proposed floor plan are not sufficient. Secondly, different alternatives for stabilizing systems
are investigated for up to 8 stories.
A variety of assumptions will have to be made, especially regarding load determination. The aim in this
regard, is to make them as general as possible, so that the results and conclusions are applicable for other
similar projects. It is desirable to have assumptions slightly to the conservative side, to include some
safety in the results.
The building is assumed to be situated in Denmark, and Danish standards are generally applied.
1.3
Methodology
The analyses of the stabilizing systems are conducted using the finite element method (FE or FEM) and
manual calculations. Where necessary, both methods are used, and different for comparison. Applicable
standards are used, but since CLT is a relatively new material, this is not possible for all design scenarios,
mainly regarding connection design. Different theories are therefore applied and analyzed, to make a
broader comparison.
1.3.1
Software
Various types of software are used throughout the project. The ones used for calculations and analyses
are listed in Table 1.1.

1.3.2
Limitations
The scope of this thesis has been limited to static loads in ULS and SLS. Dynamic loads, cyclic loads and
fire safety have not been considered, but are subjects for further analyses.

==PAGE:10==
2
Project and Case Description
An open-plan layout of an office building has been proposed by Waugh-Tristleton Arcitects in collabora-
tion with Build-in-Wood. The plan is based on a rectilinear grid, and consists structurally of a post-and-
beam type structure that carries the vertical loads. ACLT-core in the center of the floor plan shall stabilize
the structure against lateral forces. The facade only serves as a building envelope and does thereby not
contribute to the primary structural or the stabilizing system.
The material strength classes of structural elements are chosen consistently in compliance with Build-in-
Wood [1], since this will ease procurement and installation, as well as the structural analyses.
In this project, the focus is on the stability-aspect of the proposed building; and more specifically: it
proposes an answer to the question of how many and what kinds of stabilizing elements are needed, if
the building is built with 3-8 stories, as Figure 2.1 illustrates.
Figure 2.1: Considered no. of stories in project
2.1
Mass Timber
Mass timber is a prefabricated product, that is composed of compressed layers of wood using glues,
fasteners or lamination. Along with being a more uniform product, larger strengths and stiffnesses can
be obtained, than for regular structural timber, since flaws such as knots are reduced. The term вҖқmass
timberвҖқ covers a variety of products such as cross-laminated timber (CLT), glued laminated timber (GLT
or glulam) and laminated veneer lumber (LVL). Glulam is most commonly used for beams and columns.
CLT is a plate-product and is most commonly used for slabs and walls. LVL is used for a variety of
purposes. CLT has been the mass timber-product of choice for walls in this project.
2.2
Balloon Framing
Load bearing and stabilizing walls in CLT can be constructed using balloon framing or platform framing;
the two types are illustrated on Figure 2.2. Platform framing is characterized by stacked walls, that are
intersected by the floor slabs. An advantage is the low eccentricity from vertical loading on the walls
from the slabs. A disadvantage when using CLT, is the crushing loads on the slabs, since the compressive
strength perpendicular to the grain direction is much less than in the grain direction. Another disadvantage
is the shrinkage of the of the slabs from moisture changes, as the shrinkage perpendicular to the grain
direction is much larger than in the grain direction, as illustrated on the figure. For taller buildings, these
disadvantages become more pronounced, as the vertical loads increase, and the shrinkage accumulates.
Balloon framing is where the walls are continuous through the slabs, which eliminates the disadvantages
from platform framing [2]. Balloon framing is therefore beneficial for higher wood-buildings, and on
this basis, is adopted as the better choice and used for this project.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
3
==PAGE:11==

(a) Platform framing
(b) Balloon framing

2.3
Building Geometry
A plan view of the office building can be seen in Figure 2.3. The bays are divided into primary bays (a)
and service bays (b), where the latter serves as a route for installations to each floor. The installations
are led through the two shafts (d) on each side of the core (c). The geometry of the core has been revised
from the proposed layout based on the analysis in chapter 5. Dimensions regarding placement of core
walls are given, when relevant, in later analyses.
Figure 2.3: Plan view of case building with basic definitions and dimensions [mm]
A sectional view of the building with 4 stories can be seen in Figure 2.4, where floor build-ups, installa-
tions and other non-structural elements are not shown. Outermost widths of roof and facade buildup are
only shown to provide building dimensions. The same principles are valid for other building heights.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
4
==PAGE:12==
CHAPTER 2. PROJECT AND CASE DESCRIPTION
Figure 2.4: Sectional view of 4-story building with main dimensions [mm]
The service strategy for the building is to have shorter bays for the service routes, with service beams
(a) which are smaller than the primary beams (b). The slabs (c) consist of 210 mm CLT elements, and
span perpendicularly to the beams. The beams are supported by glulam columns (d), which have larger
dimensions on the lower floors. These dimensions differ with no. of stories. An additional 500 mm is
added to the total building height (e) for all considered building heights, to account for the build-up of the
roof structure. 250 mm is also added to each side of the building (f) beyond the outermost grid-lines, to
account for the build-up of the facade structure. The core consist of CLT-walls (g), which are subdivided
into wall panels of 3 m width. The panels are connected at 13 m elevation (h) to account for transportation
and production. This value is chosen w.r.t the story- and building height, to avoid intersections between
the wall-panel connections and the slab-/beam-wall connections. The same is valid for the 7- and 8-
story buildings, which have a second wall-panel connection at 26 m elevation, which is also between two
floors. Regarding the CLT-walls as stabilizing elements, there are a some connections to consider. The
walls are anchored (i) with a so-called вҖқbase hold downвҖқ connection, which stabilizes the wall against
lateral action, and transfers tensile forces to the foundation. The connection is placed on both sides
of the wall. A horizontal shear connection (j) (or base shear connection) transfers the horizontal shear
forces to the foundation. The upper wall panels at 13 m and 26 m height are connected to the panels
beneath through an anchor connection (k) and a shear connection (l), which can be made similarly to
the base connections. They can even be scaled down, as they shall transfer less forces. A vertical shear
connection (m) ensures integrity between the wall panels by transferring vertical shear forces. Wider
CLT-walls consist of multiple panels, but the types of connections are the same.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
5
==PAGE:13==
3
Theoretical Background
A selection of the main theories used throughout the project will be presented in this chapter.
3.1
Cross Laminated Timber
Cross Laminated Timber (or CLT) is the main material in this project and is presented in the following.
It is a mass timber product consisting of multiple crossing layers of mass timber plies, that are glued
together. Relevant geometrical parameters can be seen in Figure 3.1, where the subscripts L and CLT
denote lamella properties and gross element properties, respectively.
Figure 3.1: CLT element with main dimensions (modified from [6])
3.1.1
Material Properties
The individual boards can consist of different classes of structural timber. In this project, strength class
C24 is used, and the relevant strength and stiffness properties are listed in Table 3.1 in acc. with the
European Standard EN 338.
Table 3.1: Relevant stiffness and strength properties of C24 [6]
Parameter
Notation
Value
YoungвҖҷs modulus, characteristic value
Ek,L
7400 MPa
YoungвҖҷs modulus, mean value
E0,L
11 000 MPa
YoungвҖҷs modulus, perpendicular to grain, mean value
E0,90,L
370 MPa

G0,L
690 MPa

fm,L,k
24 MPa

ft,k,L,0
14.5 MPa
Compressive strength in direction of fiber, characteristic value
fc,k,L,0
21 MPa
Shear, characteristic value
fv,L,k
4.0 MPa
The relevant CLT properties are calculated with formulas by Petersen [6], which are listed in Table 3.2.
These values are used throughout the project, unless otherwise specified. The boards are considered not to
be edge-glued, as this is most common and conservative in calculations [6]. Edge-gluing also influences
the shear stress distribution in the CLT-element.

3.1.2
Failure Mechanisms
There are a variety of mechanisms for which a CLT-element can fail, of which the relevant ones for this
project are described in the following, along with how they are verified. The primary mechanisms are
failure from in-plane actions. The verifications and stresses are each shown for one in-plane direction,
where the values for the perpendicular directions are given by substituting the subscripts x and y.
In-Plane Shear
There are theoretically three modes of failure for in-plane shear. Considering an element of finite thick-
ness and uniform layer thicknesses, the failure modes can be described by the following, using a net
cross-sectional area, which is reduced from the gross area based on half of the outermost layers being
cracked, where L is the cross-sectional length:
Anet = (tCLT вҲ’tL) В· L
(3.3)
The failure modes are derived by dividing the CLT-element into an RVE-element (Representative Volume
Element) with volume b2
L В· tCLT , as presented by Petersen [6]. The RVSE-element serves as basis for the
distribution of the gross shear stresses. It can be further divided into an RVSE-element (Representative
Sub Volume Element) of volume b2
L В· tL, which includes half the thickness of two adjacent layers. The
idea is, that the RVE- and RVSE-elements are representative for stresses and strains for the whole CLT-
element.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
7
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 3. THEORETICAL BACKGROUND
Failure mode 1 (FM1) is gross failure of the element, and is simply the shear force, Vd distributed over
the net area:
П„0,d =
Vd
Anet
вүӨfv,d
(3.4)
Failure mode 2 (FM2) happens by failure of the individual layers, as every active layer shall resist the
shear stress which is transferred between the layers (derived from the RVSE-element):
П„v,d = 2П„0,d вүӨfv,xy,d
(3.5)
Failure mode 3 (FM3) can happen due to rotation in the glued connection between the boards as a result
of deformation of the gross CLT-element. As can be derived from the formula, the resistance to FM3 can
be increased by having wider boards:
П„tor,d = 3П„0,d

3.2
Eccentricity on Vertically Loaded CLT-Walls
There is no clear standardized method for calculating eccentricities for vertical loading on CLT-walls, like
there is for, e.g., concrete elements. The eccentricities are needed in order to estimate the out-of-plane
bending moments acting on the CLT-walls. The method commonly used for concrete elements is adopted,
and the principle is illustrated on Figure 3.2. It considers the slab element being deformed from vertical
load, resulting in a linearly distributed load on the bracket. Theoretically, the resultant of the reaction
force from the slab will be closer to the middle of the bracket, which means the method should be on the
safe side.
Figure 3.2: Principle for eccentricity of vertical load on balloon-frame wall
Where:

3.3
Laminated Composite Shell Theory
As a CLT-element is composed of multiple orthotropic layers, it is convenient to use laminated composite
shell theory (LCST). The theory enables a 3D-problem to be analyzed as a 2D-problem by homogenizing
the panel layers into an equivalent single layer (the method is more commonly known as вҖқequivalent
single layer theoryвҖқ). LCST is a method used by the FE-software FEM-Design for CLT-elements in their
shell-module. An advantage to the method is that the same number of equations as for an arbitrary shell
structure is needed, why the calculation time is not significantly larger. Furthermore, since shear deforma-
tions cannot be neglected for wood and mass timber structures, Reissner-Mindlin theory is implemented
[7].
The relation between stresses and strains can be seen in eq. (3.30), where f is the stresses, u is the
deformations, and K is the constitutive shell stiffness matrix. The stresses are illustrated on Figure 3.3.

3.4
Horizontal Deformation of CLT Shear Walls
The horizontal deformation of a CLT-wall can be manually calculated by dividing it into different contri-
butions as illustrated on Figure 3.4 acc. to Petersen [6]. The contributions are divided into two categories;
rigid body motions of the wall due to slip in the connections, and in-plane deformations of the wall.

3.5
Kinematic Behavior of Multiple Panel CLT-Walls
Due to the flexibility of the connections in the wall, it can behave differently, when subjected to different
loads. These behaviors can be divided into вҖқrocking kinematic modesвҖқ, that describe the deflected shape
and behavior of the wall. They depend on the parameters: geometry, stiffness and load conditions.
A method for determining the kinematic behavior of a wall applicable for platform frame structures are
provided in prEN 1995-1-1 [10] (2020 proposal for EN 1995-1-1). The method divides the kinematic
modes into coupled wall (CW), single-wall (SW) and intermediate (IN), as illustrated in Figure 3.5,
whereas only Figure 3.5a and Figure 3.5c seem relevant for a wall consisting of two panels. The black
dots indicate rotation points.
(a) Coupling (CP) mode
(b) Intermediate (IN) mode
(c) Single-wall (SW) mode
Figure 3.5: Rocking kinematic modes of multiple panel CLT-walls
It is stated, that designing a wall with CP properties can be more complex than a wall with SW properties,
why the latter is desirable. Stresses and deformations are in the wall are generally smaller, when the
behavior approaches the SW-mode.
3.6
Non-Linear Uplift Calculation
For FE-implementation of shear walls, the modeling of supports that do not take tension or compression,
i.e., compression zones and anchor supports, is essential. This is implemented in FEM-Design with a
so-called вҖқuplift calculationвҖқ, as an iterative process. In the 1st step, a support can take both tension and
compression, and it is checked, if the support is subjected to tension or compression from external load.
If it should only resist compression, and is subjected to tension, the adjacent finite elements are given a
very small tensile stiffness in the 2nd step. This process is repeated, until there is no more, or very little,
tension in the support [11]. This yields a relation between reaction force, F and displacement, e as can
be seen in Figure 3.6.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
15
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 3. THEORETICAL BACKGROUND
Figure 3.6: Relation between reaction forces and displacement with non-linear uplift calculation
3.7
Shear Capacity of Dowel-Type Fasteners
In prEN 1995-1-1 a method is given for calculating the shear capacity of dowel type fasteners in wood
structures. The method is meant as an improvement to the current DS/EN 1995-1-1. It makes some
calculations less tedious, since the current formulas are merged into a range of formulas, that shall cover
most cases, incl. timber-to-timber and steel-to-timber connections for single and multiple shear planes.
The interpolation between thick and thin steel plates is thereby discarded, and the verification of the
metal plate is included in the range of formulas, by assuming a conservative plate embedment strength
of 600 MPa (see eq. (3.51)). The theory is still based on the European Yield Model, which is based on
research by K. W. Johansen.
The design shear capacity of dowel-type fasteners in wood structures can be calculated according to
eq. (3.38) with the design dowel-effect contribution, RD,Rd, and the design rope-effect contribution,
Rrp,Rd.
Rv,Rd = kWD В· RD,Rd + Rrp,Rd
(3.38)

assuring ductility of the failure mode. Failure mode (f) in the inner members assures a good load-

between members for these. The load might not be evenly distributed by the members, as indicated by
the subscripts to FD, which relates to the adjacent members for a given shear plane. The same thing is
valid for the subscripts to the embedment depth.

3.7.1
Effective No. of Fasteners
In wood structures it is common to reduce the number of fasteners in the calculations, for a group of fas-
teners subjected to shear and placed parallel to the grain, due to the risk of splitting failure of the wood.
There is no standard for how to reduce the no. of fasteners in CLT, but different sources recommend
different approaches. The following is for fasteners mounted in the face of the CLT. Petersen [6] and
The CLT Handbook [9] states that no reduction is required, when placing screws in the face of the panel.
However, the former suggests using the value for solid timber, for screws with diameter, d < 6 mm, and
other smaller fasteners. Uibel & Blass [13] states that no reduction is needed for dowel-type fasteners in
the face of the panel. The reasoning behind not reducing the number of fasteners is because the cross-
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
19
==PAGE:27==
CHAPTER 3. THEORETICAL BACKGROUND
ing layers serves as reinforcement to splitting in the grain direction. In DS/EN 1995-1-1, CLT is not
mentioned, and in prEN 1995-1-1, it is not clear, if reduction is needed for CLT.
3.7.2
Embedment Strength of Dowels Mounted in the CLT Face
The embedment strength is defined as the yield force of the material in which a fastener is embedded over
the projected area of the fastener. From the designerвҖҷs or engineerвҖҷs perspective regarding wood struc-
tures, only the diameter of the fastener and the density of the material is needed to obtain the embedment
strength. In the following, the embedment strength in the face of CLT-elements is reviewed.
DS/EN 1995-1-1 provides the embedment strength for dowels with a diameter, d вүӨ30 mm, as in eq. (3.46),
and refers to EN 383 and EN 14358, if the design rules are not applicable for a given situation.

for dowels, screws and bolts, from the mean density of the wood material, ПҒm.

3.8
Lateral Load Distribution
Lateral load acting on a building can be distributed to the stabilizing elements using different theories
and approaches. In this project, a selection of these methods is reviewed, hereunder the tributary area
method, the rigid diaphragm method and the relative stiffness method. FE-formulation can also be used,
and the different methods can even be combined. The actual way the load will be distributed between
the walls depends greatly on the ratio between the in-plane stiffnesses of the floor diaphragms and the
stabilizing elements [9].
3.8.1
Tributary Area Method
The floor-diaphragms are considered flexible enough, for the lateral loads to be distributed to the nearest,
perpendicular wall, as indicated on Figure 3.10. This could also be described by, that the diaphragms
are considered as lateral beams, that are simply supported on the inner walls, and cantilevered on the
outermost walls.
Figure 3.10: Principle of tributary area method of lateral load in floor diaphragm
The reactions in the walls are determined by their tributary areas:

The method can be conservative for walls with low stiffness and large tributary area, relative to the
other walls in the same system. It is not necessarily conservative for stabilizing elements with different
stiffnesses.

4
Load Determination and Basis of Design
Since much of the project basis is undefined, there are some decisions that must be made in relation the
load determination. To determine the loads on the building, average realistic values are generally aimed
for. This means, that the loads should be within a reasonable range that corresponds to a realistic situation,
by e.g., disregarding extremely heavy or lightweight buildups. Generally, the dead, live and snow loads
act in the direction of gravity, the wind loads act perpendicular to the surfaces they act on, and the seismic
loads act in the gravitational center of the elements they act on. The loads are generally determined in
accordance with Eurocodes with Danish annexes. Loads from geometric imperfections are considered
negligible, since wood structures are built and placed with high precision and low tolerances.
4.1
Dead Load
The dead loads of the building are considered permanent. When estimating the dead load on a building,
information about the build-up of floors, facade, roof, etc., is normally provided beforehand. In this
project it is not given, so to estimate the dead load, different build-ups have been evaluated. To make
the analyses in this project relevant and comparable for different buildings with individual build-ups, the
dead load is defined as ranges of minimum (inferior) values, Gk,inf, and maximum (superior) values,
Gk,sup.
Build-in-Wood [1] and The CLT Handbook [9] give examples on a variety of build-ups, which are used to
estimate the surface dead loads. A floor build-up generally consists of a ceiling, a slab and a flooring. In
an office building, the ceiling usually consists of installations and cladding, which often is achieved by a
suspended ceiling, that protects the slab against fire, and dampens the acoustics in the room beneath. The
slab is the structural part, which in this project is 210 mm CLT-elements. The flooring in an office building
usually consists of a high-density layer such as concrete or dry screed for reducing sound transmission,
and a finishing layer for wear. The flooring can also be raised to reduce sound impact or to make room
for installations. The roof slab build-up is assumed to consist of roofing asphalt on a tapered insulation
layer (for leading away water), a slab and a suspended ceiling with installations.
The build-up of the facade consists of an interior lining for insulation and fire protection, a core structure
with insulation, and an external lining, that can consist of different types of cladding.
The calculation of the weight of different build-ups of the facade, floors and roof, can be seen inAppendix
A.1.
As a simplification, the same dead load is assumed over the levels, i.e., the load is the same for, e.g., long
and short spans. The characteristic surface dead loads used in this project are listed in Table 4.1. The
dead load of the cladding on the core walls is disregarded.
Table 4.1: Characteristic surface dead loads

4.2
Live Load
The building functions as offices, and the live load is determined accordingly. It is assumed, that there
is no access to the roof, which means that there is no live load on the roof, except for a small point load
that is disregarded. The same live load is, as a simplification, assumed on all floors.
Movable partition walls are treated as live loads in acc. with DS/EN 1991-1-1 [17].

4.4.2
Vertical Wind Load
Wind load on the roof can act as suction or compression. Both values are normally subdivided on the on
the roof into zones, where the load is different in each zone. The magnitude of the load in these zones
are determined by pressure coefficients, that are independent of the building height. For determination of
the pressure coefficient, the values for flat roofs are used, and sharp eaves are assumed. The extension of
these zones changes with the building height, why they need to be determined for all building heights. It
would be tedious to distribute the exact roof wind load to the structural elements for each building height,
why average values are estimated.
For compressive wind loads, the largest values are assumed on the whole roof, since these values are quite
low. This conservative assumption will not affect the structures significantly. For suction wind loads, an
average of all zones is assumed on the whole roof. The values are listed in Table 4.5.
Table 4.5: Characteristic vertical wind load pressures

Service classes are defined in DS/EN 1995-1-1 for timber structures, and accounts for the moisture content
of the wood. In service class 1, the moisture content is characterized by a temperature of 20в—ҰC, and where
the relative humidity (RH) of the surrounding air exceeds 65% only a few weeks of the year. For most
softwoods, this means, that the moisture content will not exceed 12%. In service class 2, the RH exceeds
85% only a few weeks of the year, leading to a moisture content of less than 20%. CLT is only allowed
to be used in service class 3. Only service class 1 is relevant for this project.
Normal control class is assumed in acc. with DS/EN 1995-1-1 DK NA.

5
Core Layout
The core of the building is an interface for several fields of engineering as well as architecture, resulting in
many interfering variables, why the actual design can be a puzzle. Functions such as elevators, staircases,
escape routes and installation shafts are all packed into the core, which then also has to stabilize the whole
building against lateral loads. These functions all have requirements that need to be fulfilled, why the
layout is designed through a collaborative process between the different fields. The main goal of the
structural engineer should be to have as many long continuous walls as possible in both building directions
(of course, too many walls can be unsustainable). These walls should have as few holes as possible, i.e.,
for doors and installations. The aim is to have continuous walls, which should optimally interconnect, to
stabilize the walls against buckling. Vertical load on the walls is favorable, since it stabilizes them. The
more horizontal load a wall takes, the more vertical load is thus desired for the same wall. Furthermore,
the walls should be spread out evenly and symmetrically on the plan, to reduce torsional effects.
5.1
Initial Core
The only given dimensions on the provided floor plan are between the grid lines. The initially given
layout of the core is re-drawn in Figure 5.1, where the precise placement of walls, door holes, etc. in the
core layout has been approximated by manual measurements.
Figure 5.1: Initial core plan, as proposed by WTA through Build-in-Wood [1]
Some essential information regarding the core have not been provided, hereunder span directions of slabs,
functions of the different rooms, and necessary installation holes in the walls. Some of these details has
to be defined and assumed in this project.
5.2
Revised Core
As mentioned, it is a complex task to come up with an optimal layout of the core, since many factors come
into play. By assuming that there have already been considerations about these factors, the following
parameters are assumed to be unchangeable:
1. The two stair cases must be placed in opposite ends of the core to minimize the length of the escape
routes.
2. The two installation shafts must be placed as centered as possible, and on opposite sides of the core
to provide a pathway for the installations to the two service bays.
3. The three elevators must be placed together, and as centered as possible.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
34
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 5. CORE LAYOUT
The revised core plan is shown in Figure 5.2, which is the one used in the project. The changes and the
reasoning behind the changes are explained in subsection 5.2.1 to 5.2.5. This proposed core is adapted
throughout the project, unless otherwise is stated.
Figure 5.2: Revised core plan
5.2.1
Door Holes
The distribution of horizontal loads to the walls have yet to be investigated, but it can already be estab-
lished, that there are more walls in the x-direction than in the y-direction. The door holes in the walls
in grid line 3 and 8 are therefore moved to the adjacent, perpendicular walls in grid line B and D. The
door hole between grid line 4 and 5 in grid line D is moved to between grid line 3 and 5, to get a longer
stabilizing wall in grid line D. It is considered beneficial to not have the doors too close to the edges of
the walls, to be able to restrain adjacent, perpendicular walls against buckling.
5.2.2
Stabilizing Walls
The stabilizing walls can be вҖқchosenвҖқ freely among the available walls by the structural engineer. They
play a huge role in how the core should be designed and are therefore important to be вҖқchosenвҖқ in the
early design phase, to avoid conflicts with installations, escape routes and architectural functions. The
early choice of stabilizing walls can be seen in Figure 5.3.
Figure 5.3: Early choice of stabilizing walls
Walls in the y-direction are placed close to symmetrically, which counteracts torsional effects from lateral
load. There are plenty of walls to utilize in the x-direction, why not all walls are activated. The chosen
walls in the x-direction can induce minor torsional effects. Wall 1x, 2x and 3x are interrupted by wall 2y,
why their full lengths are not utilized. This is due to the walls in the y-direction being more critical.
5.2.3
Slab Spans
It is provided, that the slabs outside of the core span in the x-direction. The walls in the y-direction take
more load than the walls in the x-direction (investigated in chapter 6), why they need more favorable
vertical load. The spans in the core are therefore chosen to be in the x-direction.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
35
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 5. CORE LAYOUT
5.2.4
Beams
An additional beam is placed in grid-line intersection 6/D, to distribute load to the stabilizing walls 3y
and 4y.
5.2.5
Elevator Placement
Wall 4y have a small tributary load area as is, while wall 2y have more; see Figure 5.4a. The spaces on
both sides of wall 3y are therefore flipped, as shown on Figure 5.4b. This evens out the vertical load
distribution between the two walls a little. The tributary load area in the new situation has decreased
for wall 2y from 19.3 m2 to 13.5 m2, and increased for wall 4y from 11.9 m2 to 17.6 m2. For wall 4y,
the tributary area has also been more evenly distributed. The remaining walls remain unaffected by this
change.
(a) Before
(b) After
Figure 5.4: Tributary areas for vertical load for wall 2y, 3y and 4y before and after movement of elevator
This simple change is favorable to the stabilizing structural system and does not cause any drastic in-
terferences with the building design. The elevators are still centered in the building, and the installation
shafts are still placed on both sides of the core.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

==PAGE:44==
6
Load Distribution
In this chapter, the basis for the distribution of the loads on the building and stabilizing systems is deter-
mined.
6.1
Horizontal Load Transmittance
It is common to distribute the load to the stabilizing elements through the floor diaphragms, which will
be done for this project also. The overall procedure for the horizontal load transmittance can be described
by the following steps, which refers to Figure 6.1.
1. The horizontal load acts on the facade elements.
2. The facade elements span vertically, where they are simply supported on the floor diaphragms, and
thereby distribute the load between the upper and lower floor diaphragms.
3. The floor diaphragm distributes the load to the stabilizing elements through in-plane shear. The
floor diaphragms are interconnected along the edges.
4. The stabilizing elements distribute the load to the foundation.
Figure 6.1: Horizontal load transmittance principle
6.2
Vertical Load Transmittance
The vertical load acting on the slabs is considered. The slabs are single spanned, transferring the load to
the perpendicular beams or CLT-walls. The beams transfer the load to the columns or CLT-walls. It would
be good for stabilization, if the shorter walls in the core did not take vertical load, but it is assumed, that
they do. They should thereby also be balloon framing-walls, to avoid crushing of the slabs. The vertical
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
37
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 6. LOAD DISTRIBUTION
loading tributary areas and lengths for the walls can be seen in Figure 6.2 and Figure 6.3 for the two
building directions.
Figure 6.2: Tributary areas of walls in x-direction
Figure 6.3: Tributary areas of wall in y-direction
6.3
Distribution of Horizontal Load
How the horizontal load gets distributed into the system can be challenging to determine correctly. There
are many methods, and one has to be considerate when choosing which one to use. For wood struc-
tures, the stiffnesses especially play a role due to slip in connections and considerably flexible structural
elements.
The challenging part of the horizontal load distribution is the in-plane stiffness of the floor diaphragms
and the stabilizing elements. Different methods for distributing the load have therefore been utilized, so a
comparison can be made. The actual load distribution will depend on some kind of relationship between
the in-plane stiffnesses of the floor diaphragms and the walls [9]. The stabilizing walls are given an ID
and illustrated in Figure 6.4.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
38
==PAGE:46==
CHAPTER 6. LOAD DISTRIBUTION
Figure 6.4: Stabilizing walls [mm]
Detailed calculations regarding the following subsections can be seen in Appendix A.5.
6.3.1
Rigid Diaphragm Method
An advantage of the rigid diaphragm method is, that it accounts for torsional effects of the diaphragm. A
disadvantage is, that it assumes a rigid diaphragm, which might not be the case for a CLT slab. It turns
out, that the shear center is only distanced 0.1 m in the x-direction and 0.14 m in the y-direction to the
geometrical center of the floor diaphragm. This results in a very small moment in the floor diaphragm,
which results in small torsional effects. The distribution of the load in the walls can be seen in Table 6.1.
Table 6.1: Horizontal load distributions with rigid diaphragm method

It can be concluded, that the walls are placed sufficiently symmetrically around the geometrical center of
the floor diaphragm for the torsional effects to be neglected, since walls perpendicular to the wind direc-
tion receive approximately no load. For load in the transverse direction, the parallel walls take an equal
amount of load, since geometry and stiffness are the same for the walls, and since they approximately are
placed symmetrically.

6.3.3
Finite Element Method
Only the walls in the y-direction is considered in this subsection, since these are the most critical. The
finite element method is utilized to estimate the influence of in-plane stiffnesses of the floor diaphragms
and the stabilizing walls; see Figure 6.6. Firstly, a model of the floor diaphragms of one floor is set up,
which is being supported by the stabilizing walls in the y-direction. Both the floors and the walls are
modeled as 210 mm CLT-elements with 7x30mm C24 layers, and the panels are 3 m wide. The walls are
modeled 4 m in height. They are supported vertically and horizontally in the bottom and are hinged to
the floor slabs. The wall panels are supported in shear with a spring stiffness of 85 kN/mm per meter.
The diaphragms are loaded with a horizontal load that totals 100 kN, that is spread out evenly along the
length of the building (100 kN/54 m/2 = 0.926 kN/m).
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
40
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 6. LOAD DISTRIBUTION
Figure 6.6: FEM-model mesh (left) and load (right)
It is investigated how the in-plane shear and tensile stiffness between the floor panels will influence the
horizontal reactions in the walls for different spring constants in Figure 6.7.
Figure 6.7: Horizontal reaction resultants [%] in walls in y-direction for different shear stiffnesses be-
tween floor panels
The more rigid the floor diaphragm is, the more evenly the load is distributed. The in-plane stiffness of
the floors is still present in the fully rigid case, why the diaphragm itself is not fully rigid. As the floor
diaphragm approaches rigidity, the load distribution should approach the one obtained with the rigid
diaphragm method. For the fully rigid case it is noted, that the trend deviates from the other results.
The stiffness value between the floor panels of 25 kN/mm/m is a realistic value and is adopted for the
following analyses. It will be now investigated how the base stiffness of the wall will influence the
horizontal reactions in the walls in Figure 6.8. In the fully rigid case, the walls are completely removed
from the model, and replaced with hinged rigid supports.

of wall supports
The lower the stiffness of the walls, the more evenly distributed the load is. For higher stiffness of the
walls, the outermost walls take more load. As stated earlier, the load distribution depends on a relation-
ship between the in-plane stiffnesses of the slabs and the walls. An investigation on the distribution for
different thicknesses is therefore made in Figure 6.9.
Figure 6.9: Horizontal reaction resultants [%] in walls in y-direction for different element thicknesses

the effects of different stiffnesses.

CHAPTER 6. LOAD DISTRIBUTION
Conclusion
The stiffer the floor is in relation to the walls, the more evenly the load is distributed. The stiffer the walls
are in relation to the floor, the more load is taken by wall 1y and 5y (outermost walls).

in the transverse building direction.

The tributary area method results in the most uneven distribution, the rigid diaphragm method results in
the most even distribution, and the finite element method results in an intermediate distribution. The trib-
utary area method yields the most conservative distribution for wall 1y and 5y, since the floor diaphragm
has large horizontal cantilevers.
For the longitudinal direction, the load is close to evenly distributed when distributing with the tributary
area method by the length, since the lengths are close. The larger the exponent to the length, the less even
the distribution is. The rigid diaphragm method yields a somewhat even distribution.
Discussion and Conclusion
A detailed design of the slabs and slab connections has not been investigated, why the actual rigidity of
the diaphragms is unknown. Due to the cantilevers, and that the floor diaphragms can not be considered
fully rigid, it seems most reasonable to distribute the load using the tributary area method, leading to
larger loads on wall 1y and 5y. This conclusion is not necessarily conservative for wall 2y, 3y and 4y.
But if wall 1y and 5y are designed to take a larger load, they will be able to take that larger load, and
thereby unload the remaining walls, as long as the system is sufficiently ductile.
It was also found, that the in-plane tensile springs between the floor panels are of much less influence than
the shear springs on the load distribution, which can be explained by, that the floor panel deformations
are dominated by shear rather than bending.
6.4
Critical Horizontal Force Type and Walls
To investigate as few scenarios as possible, it is preliminarily verified, which horizontal load will be
critical for the shear walls, along with which shear walls are critical. This is done on the basis of the
wallsвҖҷ anchor force reactions and stresses. Many parameters are equivalent for the walls, and thereby the
determination can be simplified.
6.4.1
Critical Horizontal Force Type
To estimate which horizontal force type, i.e., wind load or seismic load, will be critical for each wall,
some kind of index is needed to scale the load to the internal forces and capacities. This can be done
by a ratio between the seismic and wind load parameters for the load combinations and material. The
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

material parameters are different since the relevant wind load combination is ULS, and for seismic load
it is accidental limit state (ALS).

can be made as a condition for which load type is critical as in eq. (6.1).

for a 4-story building. However, as will be investigated in subsection 6.4.2, the walls that stabilize the
building in the longitudinal direction are not critical.
Had the building had been much wider, the seismic loading might be more critical than wind load in the
longitudinal direction. It must be emphasized, that it is a rough estimation that does not account for all
factors such as connection design, the possibility of different critical load types in different floors and
roof wind load.
Seismic loading and its effects are not further investigated in this project.

The calculation is a simplification, as does not account for skew loading on the wall due to different trib-

wall on each level. The distribution of the compression zone is also a simplified estimate. Furthermore, it
does not account for the effects from slip in the connections. Different parameters such as deformations
and stresses also need to be considered, but as a starting point, the anchor force will be the governing pa-
rameter. It is noted, that the anchor reaction has generally proven to be the critical parameter throughout
this project.

but as they also have the largest tributary areas for horizontal load, explaining why the anchor forces are
largest for these walls. The two walls yield similar results, so wall 5y is adopted as the most critical wall
for all building heights for the proposed plan layout.

7
Stabilization of Building with Proposed Core in
CLT
The buildings stabilizing system should resist horizontal forces on the building, by transferring these to
the foundations. As a starting point, the initial floor plan is investigated. To preliminarily investigate the
floor plan, some boundary conditions must be established. Dimmock, S. [5] investigated a base hold down
connection for a CLT-wall with roughly the same properties as in this project and came to the conclusion
of an anchor force capacity. The connection is quite strong, with a design tensile capacity as in eq. (7.1),
which will be used as a threshold for the anchor reaction. It consists of 44 Г— Гҳ11Г—225 mm VGS screws
(from Rothoblaas) on each side of the wall, which are inclined 45в—Ұvertically with VGU washers (also
from Rothoblaas), and mounted through 5 mm steel plates on both sides of the wall. The steel plates are
anchored to the concrete. A disadvantage of the connection is the low ductility of the fasteners, which has
been dealt with by using thin steel plates [5]. It should be possible to optimize the connection and achieve
a greater capacity by, e.g., increasing the amount of fasteners, which is investigated in section 8.2.
TRd = 1369 kN
(7.1)
According to prEN 1995-1-1, a CLT wall diaphragm should not exceed the height, h, to length, l, ratio
in eq. (7.2), which is the case for the building, if it exceeds 6 stories, By this recommendation, additional
measures must be taken for buildings of 7 stories and above.

7.1
Investigation of Proposed Floor Plan
When increasing the building height, the horizontal forces increase, both in magnitude and in amount,
and the stiffness of the walls decrease. These factors make the demands on the walls large for higher
buildings.
7.1.1
FE-Model of Walls
With the threshold for the anchor reaction, the anchor forces can be calculated, determining when the
wall is too high. A FEM-model is made of the most critical wall. The principle for the model can be
seen in Figure 7.1, and the values are provided in Table 7.1, as well as eq. (7.3). Geometry regarding
the anchor connection is adopted from Dimmock [5]. The principle of the model will be used in later
analyses also. It is noted, that however complex and precise the FE-model is, it is a resemblance based
on simplifications and assumptions, and not a replication, of a real, physical structure.

staircase not penetrating the roof structure.

7.1.3
Results
Anon-linear uplift calculation is performed for the different FEM-models, and the resulting design anchor
forces for LC4 for different numbers of stories can be seen in Figure 7.7. The scenario with no wind on
the roof was also investigated, which yielded smaller anchor forces.

==PAGE:60==
CHAPTER 7. STABILIZATION OF BUILDING WITH PROPOSED CORE IN CLT
Figure 7.7: Design anchor forces [kN] for 3-8 stories, respectively
To verify the results, a manual calculation of the anchor force using Equation 6.3 yields slightly larger
results for 5-8 story walls, (2% - 4%), a slightly smaller result for a 4-story wall (вүҲ1%), and a notably
smaller result for the 3-story wall (вүҲ12%). The compression zone might differ between the two methods,
and stiffnesses are not included in the manual calculation, so the results are reasonable.
The anchor force exceeds the threshold for more than 5 stories. To visualize the trend of the increase with
increased building height, the values are plotted in Figure 7.8.
Figure 7.8: Design anchor forces (LC4) for 3-8 stories, respectively
The trend is not linear, and the increase in anchor force gets more rapid for more floors, e.g., the an-
chor force for 7 floors is approximately 10 times the anchor force for 3 floors. This means, that for the
same floor plan layout to be used for 6 floors, the capacity of the anchor connection should be increased
approximately 20% from the assumed threshold.
It has been established, that the anchor force is exceeded for 5 stories, but it is still relevant to review the
horizontal deflection, as shown for characteristic wind load and dead load in Figure 7.9.

The same conclusion can be drawn from the analysis of the deflections as from the analysis of the anchor

7.1.4
Influence of FE-Mesh
It was found that a significantly finer mesh with 9-noded quadrilateral elements and 6-noded rectangular
elements could increase the anchor forces approximately 2%. That is a notable margin of error when
designing the walls in detail, but it is acceptable for this preliminary analysis.
7.1.5
Influence of Vertical Panel Interface Stiffness
To investigate if the panel interface could be sufficiently rigid, for the anchor force to be sufficiently small,
to comply with the threshold for 6 stories, a completely rigid panel interface was modeled. It yielded
a significant decrease of the anchor force on 5%, which is not enough to comply with the threshold.
Conclusively, even if the panel interface could be infinitely rigid, the floor layout would still not be
sufficient for stabilization for 6 stories.
7.1.6
Anchor Forces for Different Terrain Categories
As the assumed terrain category (TC) for wind load is II, it could be relevant to review the anchor forces
for different TC. There are five terrain categories in DS/EN 1991-1-4, ranging from TC 0, resulting in
the highest wind loads to TC IV, resulting in the lowest wind loads. By assuming a linear relationship
between the peak velocity pressure and the anchor reaction in the wall, the obtained anchor force can
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
54
==PAGE:62==
CHAPTER 7. STABILIZATION OF BUILDING WITH PROPOSED CORE IN CLT
be scaled to match the different terrain categories, as is done in Figure 7.10. The exact numbers are not
shown, as there is a larger margin of error in an estimation like this. Polynomial trend lines of the 2nd
order are plotted for each TC, which seem to be a reliable fit (also R2 = 0.9999 for all trend lines).
Figure 7.10: Design anchor forces (LC4) for different terrain categories
The anchor force reaction seems to double from TC IV to TC 0, putting an emphasis on the significance of
the magnitude of the horizontal load for the structural design. For TC III and IV, the anchor force threshold
is not exceeded for up to 6 stories, but for TC 0 and I, the threshold is still exceeded for 6 stories and
above. The placement of the building has a large influence on the requirements for the stabilizing system.
A building designed to be placed in a city center will probably not have sufficient capacity to stand near
a coastal area. It also puts emphasis on choosing of the right TK, and designing for the right wind load;
especially for higher buildings.
For further detailed calculations, see Appendix A.7.
7.2
Verification of 5-Story Shear Wall
It has been determined, that the floor layout is sufficient for buildings with up to 5 stories. The 5-story
shear wall is therefore examined in more detail in ULS (ultimate limit state) and in SLS (serviceability
limit state) in the following.
7.2.1
Load Determination
The same FEM-model is used as in the previous analysis for 5 stories. More loads are added, and they
can be seen in Figure 7.11, where the direction of the horizontal wind load is towards right.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

==PAGE:63==
CHAPTER 7. STABILIZATION OF BUILDING WITH PROPOSED CORE IN CLT
Figure 7.11: Characteristic load on FEM-model of 5-story wall
Furthermore, some scenarios with skew variable loading might be unfavorable to the wall. Therefore,
additional load cases are added, as shown in Figure 7.12, representing two scenarios of skewed loads. As
favorable variable loads should be discarded, the skewed load cases are investigated, even if the scenarios
are highly unlikely. In the calculations, only one load scenario can act simultaneously, e.g., live load in
Figure 7.11 can not act simultaneously with the skewed live load. Skewed load case 1 could be critical,
if the wall panels behave as a single wall. Skewed load case 2 could be critical, if the wall panels behave
as individual panels. Skew snow load is not considered, as snow load is not critical for the wall.
Figure 7.12: Characteristic skewed load on FEM-model of 5-story wall
All load combinations, as stated in section 4.6, are investigated. It is noted, that the live load is reduced
by Оұn = 0.7 for combinations with dominating live load.

7.2.2
Material
The cross section of the wall can be seen in Figure 7.13, where the x-direction is vertical. A symmetrical
cross-section eases calculations and is reasonable for a wall structure. The element consists of 7Г—30 mm
layers of C24, with two layers spanning horizontally, and 5 layers spanning vertically. The layers carry
the load in direction of their span, why more layers are needed vertically.
Figure 7.13: Cross-section of wall (horizontal cut)
7.2.3
ULS-Verification
The wall is verified for shear, buckling, compression in both directions, tension in both directions, out-of-
plane bending, and relevant combinations. It is verified for P-, M-, S- and I-load, but only the verification
for the latter is shown here, as that is the most critical for the wall. The largest relevant stresses can be
seen in Figure 7.14 (FEM-Design provides shear stresses based on the gross cross-sectional area, and
they will be increased). The normal stresses in the x-direction from LC3 is with skewed load case 1. The
wall is assumed to be restrained from buckling by the slabs and adjacent walls, resulting in an effective
buckling length equal to the floor height of 4 m. In consideration of the compressive stresses being are
larger near the compression side of the wall, as well as the previous assumption, the buckling stress is
investigated 500 mm from the side of the wall and 2 m from the bottom support.
Figure 7.14: Design stresses [MPa] in wall on ground and first floor (negative normal stresses indicate
compression)
The buckling factor is manually calculated using the gamma method and the shear-flexibility method,
and the values can be seen in eq. (7.4). The gamma method yields the most conservative result and is
adopted for the calculations. The difference is not insignificant, and the designer must be considerate,
since the shear-flexibility method automatically is used by FEM-Design.

Out-of-plane bending shows not to be a critical parameter for the wall, as out-of-plane bending combined

Using FEM, the wind load on the roof is included, as well as the effect of stabilizing vertical load (inferior

u = 30.48 mm + us,13 + ut,13 = 32.22 mm
(7.6)
The deformation in Equation 7.6 exceeds the deformation in Equation 7.5 by a significant amount. The
main reason behind this is probably the in-plane shear stiffness reduction factor, r66, which is only used
in the FEM-calculation. Also, Petersen [6] recommends considering walls that are mounted together as
separate walls, when calculating the deformation manually, which has not been done in this case.
Both values are within the deflection limit of 40 mm, which means the stiffness of the wall is sufficient
to comply with the SLS-requirement.
7.2.5
Discussion and Conclusion
The most critical wall for the building with 5 stories has been verified in ULS and in SLS by the use of a
combination of FEM-calculations and manual calculations. The walls therefore have sufficient capacity
and stiffness to stabilize the building against horizontal forces for up to 5 stories. The experiences gained
from the verification will be used for future analyses regarding CLT walls in this project, meaning some
load combinations and failure modes are redundant to verify. The load combinations with dominating
wind load yielded the largest degree of utilization. It should be safe to only consider LC4, as LC3 and LC4
yielded similar utilization degrees for the critical parameters, and since skewed live load is an unlikely
scenario. It could be argued, that the wind load on the roof in the skewed load cases should be mirrored
and replaced with the values for suction, as these are larger than the values for compression. But the
difference would be minor, and thereby negligible to the results and conclusions. The failure modes of
buckling and out-plane-bending has yielded not to be critical.
For detailed calculations; see Appendix A.8.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
59
==PAGE:67==
8
Stabilization of Building for 5+ Stories
It has been established, that the stabilizing system of the proposed floor plan is sufficient for buildings
with up to 5 stories with the provided and assumed parameters. Now it shall be investigated what will
happen, and what which initiatives to take, for the building with 6-8 stories. The possible initiatives for
the stabilizing system to be sufficient, can be broken down into two basic possibilities:
1. Increase in structural capacity and stiffness of the existing stabilizing elements
2. Redistribution of the load to additional stabilizing elements
1. can be achieved by increasing dimensions and/or material strength of the existing stabilizing elements.
Higher material strengths will not be investigated, as stresses has not shown to be a governing factor, and
since higher strengths can be less accessible. An increase in wall thickness is investigated in section 8.2.
2. can be achieved by introducing additional stabilizing elements, or by replacing the existing ones. These
elements can be shear walls in CLT, but they can also be walls in concrete or trusses in steel or wood.
These possibilities are investigated in section 8.3, 8.4 and 8.5.
It can be undesirable to have stabilizing elements of different materials, as this can introduce complexities
with regards to the load distribution due to large differences in stiffnesses.
8.1
Longitudinal Stabilization of Building
As additional measures need to be taken to achieve stabilization in the transverse (y-)direction of the
building for more than 5 floors, it should be investigated, if the longitudinal (x-)direction has sufficient
stabilizing elements. The most critical wall in the x-dir. of the building is wall 3x, as it is both the
narrowest (5.49 m), and the one with the smallest tributary area for favorable vertical load (9 m2).
The horizontal load is distributed to the walls simply based on their lengths, as this is conservative for
wall 3x; it then takes 21% of the load. 5-8 story walls are calculated using FEM-calculations, and only
the 8-story wall is shown here, since the lower walls have less critical values. The verification is similar
to that of the 5-story wall in section 7.2, so to avoid redundancy, the verification is just summarized in
Table 8.1. The slip in the joints at 13 m and 26 m is included in the deformation.
Table 8.1: Verification summary of wall 3x in the 8-story buildingвҖҷs x-direction

==PAGE:68==
CHAPTER 8. STABILIZATION OF BUILDING FOR 5+ STORIES
8.1.1
Wall Penetrations
As the wall is placed near an installation shaft, it will probably have to be penetrated to make way for the
installations at each level to the service routes. This will reduce the stiffness of the wall, and the deflection
will be increased, as seen on Figure 8.1, where 600Г—1500 mm holes are modeled 300 mm below each
level. The value in the figure is excl. contributions from slip in the horizontal connection between the
stacked wall elements at approximately 13 m and 26 m height, why the deflection limit will be exceeded.
Figure 8.1: Deflection of 8-story wall with holes
As the wall stiffness is decreased, it would be recommendable to consider a load distribution, that takes
this into account. Thereby the load in the wall would be decreased, and it might comply with the deflection
limit. The increase in stresses due to the penetrations were found to be insignificant.
Alternatively, a larger amount of the walls in the x-direction could be activated, as illustrated on Fig-
ure 8.2, where wall 2x and 3x are made longer. This should definitely be sufficient for the longitudinal
stabilization of the building. Three of the walls in the y-direction can be moved half a wall thickness,
105 mm, and their widths, and thereby their capacities, are not decreased. This causes insignificant asym-
metry of the floor plan, that can be neglected.
Figure 8.2: Tributary areas with increased wall-length in longitudinal building direction
For stabilization, there should be a sufficient number of walls to вҖқchoose fromвҖқ in the longitudinal direc-
tion of the building for up to 8 stories. Additional measures should therefore not be needed.
For more detailed calculations; see Appendix A.9.
8.2
Increased Wall Thickness
By increasing the thickness of the stabilizing walls, the core design might be sufficient for higher build-
ings. It is investigated, if an increase in wall thickness is sufficient for the stabilizing system for a 6-story
building, without additional stabilizing elements. Increase in number of layers (wall 2, Figure 8.3b) and
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
61

The walls can withstand the compressive stresses, but wall 1 and 2 fail to withstand the tensile and shear
stresses. Wall 3 withstands all the stresses and should therefore have sufficient capacity. Wall 2 might
be able to withstand the stresses, if a higher strength class for the lamellas is chosen, and if a higher
gross-shear strength is used, which some manufacturers might provide according to Petersen [6].
For characteristic loading, the deflection can be seen in Figure 8.7. The largest contributions to the
deformation have yielded to be shear and bending, which changes with the gross cross-sectional area and
net moment of inertia. The latter is the largest contributor, and is identical for wall 1 and 2, why wall
3 differs more. Further, the shear stiffness increases with the gross wall thickness and effective shear
modulus, why wall 3 also resists shear deformations better.

8.2.1
Anchoring of Wall
As was determined in chapter 7, the anchor reaction exceeds the adopted threshold for the 6-story build-
ing. It shall therefore be investigated, if a sufficiently strong anchor connection is obtainable. Wall 3 is
considered, and the anchor reaction, that the connection shall transfer, is calculated as seen in Figure 8.8.
Figure 8.8: Anchor reaction [kN] (LC3)
The anchor connection type as investigated by Dimmock [5] is considered with the increased lamella
thickness of wall 3 and an increased amount of fasteners, as is illustrated on Figure 8.9. The CLT wall is
anchored to the foundation (not shown) through 5 mm S355 steel plates on both sides of the wall. Each
plate is mounted to the CLT-wall with 11Г—5 45в—Ұinclined Гҳ11Г—225 mm VGS-screws with VGU1145
washers.

CHAPTER 8. STABILIZATION OF BUILDING FOR 5+ STORIES
Figure 8.9: Anchor connection [mm] (connection to foundation not shown)
The connection is verified against various failure modes of the wood, fasteners and steel plates, and the
utilization degrees of each mode can be seen in Table 8.4.

Increasing the number of fasteners and the thickness of the CLT-lamellas has increased the capacity of
the anchor connection, such that it can withstand the anchor force from lateral load on a 6 story building
without changing the stabilizing system. Tension in the wood or shear in the fasteners yield the lowest
capacities and will likely be the cause of failure. This verification did not include FE-calculations, and a
more detailed investigation is advisable if the connection is to be used. The connection of the anchors to
the concrete should also be investigated, but it should be possible to make this design, as the wood-part
of anchor connections often is the more critical case.
Discussion
The lateral deflection of the wall appears to be the governing factor for the wall, but it might be smaller
when calculating the stiffnesses in detail; the stiffness of the panel interface connection will most probably
be larger, since it should transfer larger stresses, and the stiffness of the base shear connection might
also be underestimated. Theoretically, the wall thickness could be increased further for more stories.
However, if the walls are too thick, they will interfere with the space and functions of the building, which
is not desirable. There is also the aspect of manufacturing and availability, for which the CLT-dimensions
should be within reasonable limits.
For more detailed calculations; see Appendix A.9.

8.3
Additional CLT Shear Walls
In this section it shall be investigated, how the stabilizing system can perform, if additional CLT-walls
are implemented to the proposed floor plan. Walls obstruct the flexibility of the floor plan architecturally,
and as few as possible, is desirable. When вҖқchoosingвҖқ the placement of the additional walls, the focus is
therefore both on how the load will be distributed, but also on how the floor plan will be obstructed. In
the proposed initial floor plan, the two outermost core walls are most critical, due to the cantilevering of
the floor diaphragm. To unload these, the additional walls therefore need to be placed towards or in the
gables. Four alternatives has therefore been considered, as is illustrated in Figure 8.10. The procedure in
the following analyses has been as follows:
1. Determine how many floors is possible with alt. 1.
2. Determine how many floors is possible with alt. 2.
3. Determine which of alt. 3-4 is best, and then how many floors is possible with the best of the two.
(a) Alternative 1 and 2
(b) Alternative 3 and 4
Figure 8.10: Stabilizing system alternatives with additional CLT walls
More configurations are possible, but the presented ones are considered to be the optimal choices.
For each alternative, the following procedure is done to evaluate the floor plan, for the considered no. of
stories:
1. Determine critical wall by manually calculating the anchor reactions for all walls (using Equa-
tion 6.3).
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
67
==PAGE:75==
CHAPTER 8. STABILIZATION OF BUILDING FOR 5+ STORIES
2. Evaluate anchor reaction and deformation (incl. slip contributions) for critical wall using FEM*
for LC4 (dom. wind load).
3. Evaluate stresses** for critical wall for LC3 and LC4 using FEM* for the no. of stories that com-
plies with the anchor and deformation threshold.
*Non-linear uplift FEM-calculation
**Shear, tensile and compressive stresses are evaluated, as these have shown to be the most critical.

far more critical, unless using a different method to distribute the horizontal load.

8.3.3
Alternative 3 and 4
If no additional walls within the floor space is desired, the possibility of adding walls to the gables should
be investigated. This will however largely restrict the placement of windows in the gables.
If the tributary area method is used to distribute the load, the initial core walls will not be unloaded suffi-
ciently, and the same results as for alternative 2 would be obtained, as the load would not be distributed
away from the critical wall. An elastic load distribution therefore needs to be assumed, for the longer,
additional walls to be effective and take up more loads than the core walls. This will also utilize the
additional walls better.
An elastic load distribution based on the reduced stiffness of the walls, by accounting for the joints, by
l1.5, as suggested by Petersen [6], results in a load distribution that unloads the walls sufficiently. The
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

8.4
Bracing in Wood or Steel
Different types of bracing are commonly used in wooden buildings. In this project, it can be implemented
in the stabilizing system to work together with the CLT-shear walls, and thereby contributing to the overall
capacity of the system. As opposed to stabilizing walls, bracing offers the advantage of not obstructing
and taking up a space completely; if placed in exterior walls, it is still possible to have windows or doors.
Two suggestions of bracing in wood as presented by Build-in-Wood [1] are considered, along with the
alternative of using steel bracing, as presented in Figure 8.14.
(a) Multiple story diagonals, wood
(b) Inverted V-bracing, wood
(c) Cross-bracing, steel
Figure 8.14: Considered bracing types
A large difference between using wood and steel for bracing is, that the wood diagonals can take com-
pression, where the steel diagonals only take tension. This is under the assumption, that a given steel
diagonal is sufficiently slender, so that it will deform elastically, and not plastically, transversely to its
axial plane, when subjected to compressive forces.
The bracing types of multiple story diagonals in Figure 8.14a and inverted V-bracing (also known as
Chevron bracing) in Figure 8.14b are considered for wood. Option (a) could give rise to more complexi-
ties in connection design and interfaces than option (b) due to non-uniformity along the building height.
Option (a) provides more space for windows than option (b), but when designing an office building, it is
often architecturally desirable to have uniformity in the facade between stories. Based on this assumption,
option (b) is adopted as the better choice, and is investigated.
An essential consideration when utilizing multiple types of stabilizing elements in the same system (e.g.,
CLT shear walls and bracing) is the load distribution, due to the potentially large differences in in-plane
stiffness. Firstly, the max. load the wall can take is estimated, and then the iterative design process in
Figure 8.15 is followed, for designing the bracing system. Since the load distribution is based on stiffness;
changing a dimension in the bracing system changes the stiffness, which changes the amount of load it is
subjected to, why the process is iterative.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
71
==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 8. STABILIZATION OF BUILDING FOR 5+ STORIES
Figure 8.15: Design process of bracing system
The first step is determining the max. amount of horizontal load the shear walls can take. The critical
parameter is considered to be the anchor reaction, which is calculated manually for an 8- and 7-story wall.
The amount of load the wall can take, before the anchor force capacity is exceeded, is determined itera-
tively, using Equation 6.3. The calculation yields, that the wall can max. take the amount of horizontal
load as presented in Table 8.9.
Table 8.9: Max. amount of horizontal load in wall
No. of floors

8.4.1
Bracing in Wood
The principle for the static system of the wood bracing can be seen in Figure 8.17. The bracing elements
are hinged in the ends with 120 kN
mm springs, corresponding to 8Г—20 mm-fasteners in double-shear-plane
timber-to steel connections in both elements in the connection.
Figure 8.17: Static system for wood bracing
It has been iteratively determined, that bracing alternative 1 is sufficient for up to 7 stories, and alt. 2 for
up to 8 stories, why those heights are considered for the systems in the following.
Load Distribution
The stiffness of the wall is calculated manually, and is estimated to be Kwall = 539 kN/m/m for 7
stories and Kwall = 382 kN/m/m for 8 stories, by including deformation contributions from bending,
shear, rigid body rocking, rigid body sliding and coupling slip. The stiffness of the bracing system is
more complex to calculate, but by considering relative displacements, the stiffness can be estimated by
assuming the following relation, where the deformations for the different systems are based on the same
load:
Kwall
Kbracing
= ubracing
uwall
(8.1)
Where:
Kbracing = stiffness of bracing system
ubracing = deformation of bracing system
uwall = deformation of wall
Subjecting the wall and the bracing to a load of 1 kN/m along each story, the deformations in Figure 8.18
are obtained.

The same finite element model of the diaphragms with the same load as investigated earlier in subsec-
tion 6.3.3 is used with 210 mm CLT slabs with a shear stiffness of 25 000 kN/m/m between them. The
walls and bracing elements are modeled as linear spring supports with the estimated stiffnesses. The
resulting load distributions are shown in Figure 8.19.
Figure 8.19: Horizontal reaction resultants [%] in walls and bracing systems

the bracing supports and 14.2% in the wall supports, which is beneath the max. value of 15%.
For alt. 2, the bracing support takes an average of 13.3%, and the wall supports, 9.4%, which is beneath
the max. value of 11%. The bracing elements thereby unload the walls sufficiently in both scenarios.

8.4.2
Bracing in Steel
When using steel as bracing, diagonals are needed in both directions in each story (or just along the whole
height of the bracing system), since only the diagonal in tension is activated, when the system is subjected
to lateral load, as mentioned. That is why the cross-bracing in Figure 8.14c is considered for the steel
bracing. The principle for the static system used in the calculations can be seen in Figure 8.21.
Figure 8.21: Static system for steel bracing
As for the wood bracing, it has been iteratively determined, that alt. 1 is sufficient for up to 7 stories, and
alt. 2 is for up to 8 stories.
Load Distribution
The load is distributed as for the bracing in wood, using relative displacements. The values for the walls
have been obtained in subsection 8.4.1, and the values for the steel bracing can be seen in Figure 8.22.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

Figure 8.22: Relative deformations [mm] of bracing systems
The relative stiffness of the systems are calculated using eq. (8.1), obtaining Kbracing = 546 kN/m/m
for alt. 1 and Kbracing = 477 kN/m/m for alt. 2. The same diaphragm model as for the wood bracings
are modified with the steel-bracing stiffnesses, obtaining the load distribution in Figure 8.23.
Figure 8.23: Horizontal reaction resultants [%] in walls and bracing systems
It is noted, that the distribution is highly similar to the one obtained with wood bracing.
For alt. 1, 14.3% is taken by each bracing support, and 14.3%<15% in each wall support. For alt. 2,
13.5% is taken by each bracing support, and 9.2%<11% in each wall support. The bracing elements
thereby unloads the walls sufficiently.
The dead loads are the same as for the wood bracing, except for the structural dead load (see Figure 8.20),
and the wind load is almost the same (less than 2% deviation), why the loading is not shown here.
