8.4.5
Connections
The gross structural elements have been verified in ULS and SLS for horizontal loading on the building,
but the connections shall also be briefly considered, before the dimensions of the wood are concluded.
This is due to the spacing requirements for fasteners and reduction in cross-section from perforation in
the wood. Double shear connections with a recessed 10 mm steel plate with M20 8.8 bolts are assumed
as basis for the connections. The steel plate shall transfer the load between the gross elements through the
fasteners but is not designed in this project. For calculating the capacity of the connection, an effective
amount of fasteners in the direction of the grain is considered in acc. with Munch-Andersen et al. [28]
as in eq. (8.6) to avoid splitting of the wood.

The capacities of the bolts are calculated, and the results are summarized in the following. The wood

The beam connection in Figure 8.27 is subjected to a design compressive force of 457 kN, yielding a

The column connection in Figure 8.28 is subjected to a design tensile force of 502 kN, yielding a degree
of utilization of 96%.
More fasteners could be added to lower the utilization degrees. It is noted, that the large glulam dimen-
sions could consist of two subelements that are screwed together, which should not be interfere with the
considered connections. It can be concluded, that there is sufficient space in the elements for the joints.
8.4.6
Discussion and Conclusion
Bracing alternative 1 (bracing in single 6 m bay in both gables) is sufficient for up to 7 stories, and
alternative 2 (bracing in two 7.5 m bays in each gable) is sufficient for up to 8 stories, to stabilize the
building, both with wood and steel. By implementing the bracing systems, the CLT core-walls can be
unloaded enough to still to be able to contribute to the stabilizing system.
It must be emphasized, that buildability, installation complexity, etc. have not been considered in detail
for the connections. The objective was to examine, if the obtained element dimensions were large enough
to have room for the connections.
For more detailed calculations; see Appendix A.10.
8.5
Concrete Core
Should fire regulations prohibit the usage of combustible materials (hereunder wood) in the core, concrete
can be used as an alternative. Should it be undesired to have additional stabilizing elements on the floor
plan, a concrete core could be necessary for 6- to 8-story buildings. Using concrete will increase the time
spent on the building site, especially if in-situ concrete is used, as well as the environmental footprint of
the building [1]. A concrete core with and without pre-stressed reinforcement is briefly examined in this
section. Both types are provided with reinforcement meshes, as illustrated on Figure 8.29. Prefabricated
concrete walls are anchored together with reinforcement rods, that are placed through corrugated steel
ducts; see Figure 8.29a. Post-tensioning the walls gives them a much larger resistance to lateral loads; see
Figure 8.29b, where P is the post-tensioning force. The stacked prefabricated walls are interconnected
by the tensioning lines, that are anchored to the base of the bottom wall. The lines are then stressed after
placement.
(a) Anchored wall
(b) Post-tensioned wall
Figure 8.29: Concrete wall principles
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
80
==PAGE:88==
CHAPTER 8. STABILIZATION OF BUILDING FOR 5+ STORIES
The process has been to investigate the max. no. of stories using prefabricated anchored walls, after
which the remaining no. of stories up to 8 is investigated using post-tensioned walls.
Beyond what is considered in the following subsections, the walls are provided with minimum reinforce-
ment in acc. with DS/EN 1992-1-1 [29], by considering them as walls and deep beams. Both types of
walls are provided with Y12/350 mm vertically and Y12/250 mm horizontally on both sides of the walls.
The shear capacity is verified based on these reinforcement meshes but is not shown here.
8.5.1
Distribution of Load
As the concrete walls are much more rigid than the floor-diaphragms in CLT, the load is distributed
using the tributary area method. The critical wall in consideration is therefore the outermost wall in the
buildingвҖҷs y-direction, wall 5y (stabilizing walls are defined on Figure 6.4).
8.5.2

Prefabricated reinforced concrete shear walls are considered. They are manually examined in a spreadsheet-
calculation, with the principles shown on Figure 8.30. The principles follow the procedure, as presented
by Bochersen, E. [30].

8.5.4
Results
With 200 mm prefabricated concrete walls of strength class C25, the core of the building is sufficient as
is for up to 4 stories, and possibly 5 with optimization, or if a stronger anchor connection is designed.
Anchoring is not a necessity for 3 stories.
With 260 mm prefabricated post-tensioned walls of strength class C35, the core of the building is suffi-
cient as is for up to 8 stories. Optimization regarding wall thickness, strength class, etc., for a smaller no.
of stories, has not been investigated.
Shear reinforcement against sliding was found not to be necessary for any of the walls. For approx. 10%
larger wind load, it might be necessary for the walls without post-tensioning. The shear capacities of the
walls were found not to be a critical factor.
For more detailed calculations, and also calculations regarding minimum reinforcement and shear capac-
ity; see Appendix A.11.
It is emphasized, that the calculations regarding concrete have not been detailed, and that aspects such
as relaxation, creep, cracking and deflection are subjects for further analyses. Furthermore, the seismic
load is increased when using concrete walls instead of CLT walls, due to the increase in mass, which was
not considered.
8.6
Summary
Different stabilizing systems have been investigated. A summary is tabulated in Table 8.14, where the di-
mensions indicate amountГ—width, and where X indicates the max. no. of stories of, which the stabilizing
structure is sufficient. If using a concrete core, post-tensioning is needed for 5-6 to 8 stories.

9
Vertical Shear Connection
The vertical shear connection between the CLT-panels in the walls is investigated in the following; see
Figure 9.1. The connection transfer shear forces between the panels, ensuring that the wall-panels ap-
proach acting as a single unit, why the stiffness of the connection is of high importance.
Figure 9.1: Vertical shear connection between wall panels
9.1
Performance Requirements of Connection
It is an iterative process to estimate the design of the connection, as the stiffness and shear flow affect
each other. There are different ways to approach this problem. In this investigation, the shear force and
stiffness are firstly estimated as a starting point for designing the connection. Other important factors are
price, availability and installation procedure.
The building with 5 stories is considered. As a boundary condition, the design anchor force may not
exceed TRd, and the compressive stress in the compression zone may not exceed fc.0.d. Furthermore, the
lateral deflection of the wall should not exceed umax =
h
500. The required stiffness of the connection is
determined based on these conditions, and the values can be seen in Table 9.1.
Table 9.1: Initial performance requirements for vertical shear connection
Parameter

umax

The reason the stiffness is of high importance, is because it affects many aspects of the wall design, as
indicated in Figure 9.2. Most of the parameters benefit from a higher stiffness, except for the connection
itself, that will then attract larger forces.

9.1.1
Stiffness in the Ultimate Limit State
The lower the stiffness is, the higher the anchor force will be, since the panels will start to act as individual
walls, leading to a smaller moment arm for between the reactions. The wall is modeled with different
shear stiffnesses for LC4 in Figure 9.3. The compressive stresses in the compression zone of the wall
were found not to exceed the capacity for any of the shear stiffnesses.

required stiffness for this parameter is 5 kN

becomes less pronounced for larger values of the stiffness, as illustrated by the compressive reaction in
the left panels; this is investigated in subsection 9.1.4.
The shear stresses in the wall are a highly relevant factor, since they will increase with decreased stiffness,
as seen on Figure 9.4, which are taken 100 mm above the base, to reduce the effect of peak stresses.

9.1.3
Shear Flow
Knowing the minimum required stiffness, the shear flow can be determined. Since the required stiffness
is relatively low, the connection will probably be based on the required strength of the connection, and the
actual stiffness will thereby be higher than required. A higher stiffness will attract more forces, so to get
an initial idea about the shear flow, the stiffness is overestimated in a FE-model, causing the shear flow
to be conservatively high. The shear flow is investigated for the required stiffness, a larger stiffness, and
a rigid connection for LC4; see Figure 9.10. The rigid connection serves as a limit to what the maximum
obtainable value is.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

(LC4) for different vertical shear stiffnesses
Unrealistically large values of the shear flow are obtained at the bottom of the panel due to singularities,
why they are ignored. As mentioned, the stiffness of the connection will most probably exceed the mini-
mum value. The design shear flow from the larger stiffness is therefore considered, and the largest value,
disregarding the support singularity, is adopted, as in eq. (9.4).

9.1.5
Discussion and Conclusion
Collectively, it is indicated, that higher stiffness values are desirable. A minimum value of the vertical
shear stiffness in eq. (9.5) is adopted as the minimum threshold. This value is perhaps a bit high, since it
is based on peak stresses. Considering, that it can be difficult to predict the actual stiffness, and that the
stiffness is reduced for larger loads, it should be a reasonable value to aim for.
Ks,min = 50 kN
mmm
вҲ’1
(9.5)
The anchor connection is subjected to a smaller force for a higher shear stiffness. Ultimately this means,
that the anchor connection can be scaled down, although this would reduce its stiffness, which changes
the behavior of the wall; emphasizing that it is a complex issue to optimize. One parameter that does not
benefit from a higher stiffness is the vertical shear connection itself, as the higher stiffness will make it
attract more forces.
9.2
Load Distribution Between Fasteners
It is common to assume a plastic distribution of the load between the fasteners in a shear connection, as
illustrated in Figure 9.13. An internal moment arm, am is needed to absorb the moment from the eccentric
load between the panels, where a longer internal moment arm leads to a smaller force in the fasteners that
resist the moment. By the use of this principle, a longer bracket or plate is favored over a shorter one, to
minimize the total amount of fasteners needed.
Figure 9.13: Plastic load distribution in shear connection between wall panels
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
92
==PAGE:100==
CHAPTER 9. VERTICAL SHEAR CONNECTION
A small investigation is done through the use of the FE-method, to verify this principle. Steel plates of
10 mm thickness are modeled, as shown on Figure 9.14, where a = e = 50 mm and F = 1 kN
m . The
models shall resemble steel plates in a shear connection between two wall panels, where the fasteners are
modeled as simple supports, and where the load is the reaction from the fasteners in the adjacent wall
panel. The reactions are given in [N] and are approx. symmetric. The assumption of letting the outermost
fasteners take the eccentricity moment seems reasonable and is adopted for future investigations.
Figure 9.14: FEM-models and reactions of 10 mm steel plates with 5, 10 and 15 fasteners modeled as
simple supports
The largest difference between vertical reactions in each plate is between the outer and the middle fas-
teners. This difference becomes larger for the longer plates. The influence of plate stiffness and sup-
port stiffness are therefore investigated on the load distribution between the fasteners taking shear. By
changing the supports to 5 вҲ’40 kN
mm springs, in the model with 10 fasteners, the load is close to evenly
distributed; < 1% difference between largest and smallest reaction, in comparison with stiff supports,
where the difference is вүҲ19%.
The sum of the loads is manually calculated with eq. (9.6) with nf no. of fasteners in a single column,
from the external force, F.
Fsum = F

The FEM-calculation is made for different no. of fasteners, making the outermost fasteners take only
horizontal reactions, and the results are plotted with the manually calculated results in Figure 9.15. There
is very good correlation between the two, why eq. (9.6) is concluded sufficiently accurate. With the used
parameters, a few amount of fasteners is needed before the sum of the internal forces is significantly
reduced.

93
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 9. VERTICAL SHEAR CONNECTION
Figure 9.15: Graph showing the sum of fastener forces vs no. of fasteners for a = e = 50 mm
By rearrangement of eq. (9.6), the plot shows that the sum of the internal forces can be significantly
reduced by letting the distance between the outermost fasteners be 15-20 Г— the external force eccentricity.
Each connecting plate in the shear connection between the wall panels should therefore preferably at least
have that length. Of course, if the plate becomes too long to handle, it could be difficult to install.
Conclusion
The load distribution between a single column of fasteners has been analytically investigated. Based
on the conducted analyses, it is safe to use the proposed plastic distribution (in Figure 9.13) of the load
between the fasteners. The flexibility ensures, that the load is approximately evenly distributed between
the fasteners taking the vertical force.
For more detailed calculations; see Appendix A.12.
The analysis was based on constant values of e and a, but the conclusion should be applicable for other
values as well.
9.3
Preliminary Joint Alternatives
There are many ways an in-plane shear joint can be designed. The joint in this project shall transfer
large shear forces and ensure the integrity of the wall between the panels. Dowel-type connections are
therefore accepted as a good choice for the connection. Edge distance and spacing of fasteners are of
interest for evaluating the joint type, which are illustrated on Figure 9.16.
Figure 9.16: Relevant distances on wall elevation
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
94
==PAGE:102==
CHAPTER 9. VERTICAL SHEAR CONNECTION
A variety of design alternatives of dowel-type connections, as can be seen in Figure 9.17, is investigated,
to get an idea about the performances. It is desirable to have as little work as possible on the building
site, to reduce construction/installation time. Common for all the joints is, that half of the joint can be
installed at the factory, if tolerances are sufficiently tight. Joint (d), (g), (h) and (i) require pre-drilling,
which can also be done on the factory, but it also gives rise to installation complexities with tolerances
and greater risk of errors. For joint (e) and (f), pre-drilling is recommended by the manufacturer of the
screws.
Figure 9.17: Different alternatives of vertical shear connections
The needed number of fasteners for complying with the minimum values of resistance (nxy) and stiffness
(Kser,min) is stated in Table 9.4, which are calculated based on the formulas in prEN 1995-1-1 for dowel-
type fasteners. The highest possible fastener distances for complying with the criteria are used. The values
in this section are based on the effective number of fasteners, nef = n, as evaluated in subsection 3.7.1.
It could be argued, that a reduced value of nef should be used, as the joint is placed close to the edge of
the panel. PetersenвҖҷs [6] method of calculating the embedment strength has been used, as it yields the
lowest value. The methods by Uibel and Blass [13] and Tuhkanen et al. [15] are not applicable for the
adopted CLT cross-section.

9.3.1
Joint (a)-(c)
These joints are variations with SBDs (self-drilling dowels) from Rothoblaas with recessed holes in the
wood, in which metal plates of steel or aluminum are inserted. There is no need for pre-drilling with
SBDs, since they supposedly can drill through the wood and the metal using their relatively sharp tip.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
96
==PAGE:104==
CHAPTER 9. VERTICAL SHEAR CONNECTION
A thread closest to the tip does the screwing but might be damaged after drilling through the first metal
plate. The SBD might get stuck in the plate and thereby damage the wood internally. Aluminum is softer
than steel, which should make it easier to drill through. Joint (a) has only two shear planes, which results
in lower stiffness and strength than (b) and (c). Joint (b) has four shear planes, why the connection has
highest stiffness and capacity in comparison to (a) and (c). It might be tough to drill through two plates,
which is the inspiration for (c). Using shorter SBDs, and drilling from each side of the wall, only one
metal plate has to be drilled through by each SBD, making the installation easier than joint (b). Joint (c)
has 2x2 shear planes, why the connection has high stiffness, as a result of a larger number of fasteners
needed.
A limitation to the SBDs is, that they are only available with a diameter of 7.5 mm.
The initial hypothesis was that SBDs would be the best choice for the joint. But a small test was con-
ducted, of the SBDs ability to drill through metal. A 19 mm aluminum plate (of unknown strength and
hardness) was placed between two pieces of wood, and the SBDs was drilled through the setup. Two
SBDs where successfully drilled through, but it was very tough work. It might also have been some
tough aluminum, but based on this test, the idea of using SBDs were discarded.
9.3.2
Joint (d)
Joint (d) consists of steel plates, that are recessed into the outside of the wall and mounted with bolts.
Bolts can be made in many sizes, why the strength can be high, even though the connection only has two
shear planes. However, with high strength of one fastener, fewer fasteners are needed, and the stiffness
will thereby be limited. Using this type of joint, the amount of fasteners needed, will most probably
be determined by the required stiffness, rather than the required strength. Joint (d) is the only joint that
extends out of wall surface, but this will most probably not cause complexities.
9.3.3
Joint (e)
Joint (e) consists of steel plates recessed into the outside of the wall, which are mounted with VGS
screws from both sides. VGS-screws have a high capacity, which can be utilized in this type of joint.
The connection has only one shear plane per fastener, which is significantly reflected by the strength and
stiffness. This is also why an additional screw is needed in each wall panel. An advantage to the joint is,
that it should be easy to install.
9.3.4
Joint (f)
Joint (f) consists of two plates of laminated wood, e.g Baubuche [32], recessed into the wall and mounted
with VGS screws. Baubuche is a hardwood laminated veneer lumber product similar to LVL, that has
an advantageously high density compared to other wood products. High strength screws can be utilized
for this type of joint, since there are no metal plates to drill through. When using wood as connection
plates in the joint, a high strength is needed, since wood pieces will have to transfer the same forces as the
CLT-wall. The wood pieces should therefore be as thin as possible, to reduce reduction of the CLT cross-
section. The wood pieces should still have sufficient embedment strength. The high density is not enough
to counteract the fact that it is a timber-to-timber joint, resulting in an intermediately stiff joint. Using
screws, the joint should be easy to install. As an alternative to Baubuche, glued laminated timber or LVL
could be used, which consequently would reduce the strength and stiffness due to the lower densities.
9.3.5
Joint (g)-(i)
This type of joint is an alternative to joint (a)-(c). The joints consist of smooth dowels (S235 STA dowels
from Rothoblaas) with recessed steel plates. There is a possibility, that it could be troublesome to get the
dowel through for connection type (h), why the tolerances should be tight. Joint (i) is meant as a solution
to this, where a dowel is inserted from both sides. This does not change the stiffness, if the number of
dowels used is doubled, but the strength will be less. Joint (g) is the strongest and stiffest of the three
joints, and is the best choice, if installation is obtainable. If not, joint (i) is the better choice. Another
advantage of dowels is the simplicity of production and the availability.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
97
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 9. VERTICAL SHEAR CONNECTION
9.3.6
Final Thoughts and Conclusion
For joint (a), (b), and (f), it is only necessary to access the wall from one side during installation, which
might be favorable in certain scenarios.
Alternative (e) has no properties that exceeds the other alternatives, why it is discarded. In relation to
strength and stiffness needed to comply with the thresholds, joint (b), (d), (f) and (h) are desirable, where
joint (d) and (f) are discarded due to the lower stiffnesses, and joint (b) is discarded due to installation
complexities. Considering only the obtainable properties, the dowels and SBDs with two recessed metal
plates ((b), (c), (h) and (i)) show the greatest potential when considering both capacity and stiffness, when
using fasteners that penetrate both plates ((b) and (h)).
Joint (h); long dowels with two recessed steel plates, is adopted to be the most promising connection
type; a low number of fasteners are needed to achieve sufficient strength and stiffness, and installation
should not be too complex.
9.4
Dowel Diameters
Joint alternative (h) has yielded to be the most promising connection type in the preliminary analysis,
which was based on M12-dowels. It shall now be investigated, if the use of M16 or M20 dowels are
more efficient with respect to stiffness, strength and distances in Table 9.6. STA dowels from Rothoblaas
are still considered, where the M12 dowel has a weaker capacity, S235, than the other two, S355.

The larger diameter, the larger edge distance is needed, which results in an internal moment in the con-
nection from the eccentricity. The resulting moments are not very large, and it should not be a problem
for the connection to resist it for any of the diameters, if sufficiently long recessed steel plates are used.
Another argument for the desirability of a smaller edge distance, is that a smaller recess for the steel plate
will be needed, which lowers installation complexity. In this regard, it is better to use smaller dowels.
Considering the needed number of fasteners, to comply with the design shear flow, nxy, fewer larger
dowels are needed than the smaller ones. Another relevant parameter to consider is the shear capacity
per area. Since the bolts are placed in a single line, the width goes out of the equation, leaving only
the vertical distance, and thereby the shear capacity per length (max. joint capacity in Table 9.6). M16
dowels have the max. value in this respect. It should be mentioned, that using the same strength class
for the dowels yielded that M12 dowels had the largest capacity per length (not shown). Based on these
considerations, the optimal choice is M16 STA dowels.

Detailed calculations regarding this section can be seen in Appendix A.14.
9.5
Design of Connection
A double shear connection with M16 STA dowels has been determined as the best choice for the connec-
tion. It shall now be designed in more detail.
9.5.1
Placement of Steel Plates
It is desirable to aim for the ductile failure modes (C) or (D). prEN 1995-1-1 suggests minimum required
member thicknesses for obtaining these. These min. thicknesses can вҖқunfortunatelyвҖқ not be obtained
with the determined parameters; mainly because the dowel diameters are too large. Therefore, the aim
is to avoid the failure modes with large non-uniformity in the load-displacement relationship between
members: (E), (F) and (G). The failure modes are plotted as a function of the embedment thickness of
the outer member in Figure 9.18 with 10 mm steel plates, and otherwise same parameters as in previous
analyses. Failure mode (B) has been removed from the plot, as it is much larger than the remaining modes
(Rv,k,B = 192 kN no matter the wood thickness).
Figure 9.18: Failure modes [kN] vs outer member thickness [mm]
The failure mode is governed by the lowest value, why failure mode (A) (embedment failure of the outer
and inner CLT-members) is governing for t1 = 22.6 вҲ’51.8 mm. A value in this range is therefore
desirable.
For ductile failure modes to be governing for the connection, a smaller dowel diameter must be used.
An outer member thickness of t1 = 45 mm is adopted, roughly corresponding to just outside the outer
quarter-points of the CLT-element.
9.5.2
Thickness of Steel Plates
With the value of t1 determined, the center of the steel plates is now assumed to be 50 mm from each side
of the wall. It shall be investigated, which steel plate thickness to use, with the goal of obtaining a ductile
failure mode, i.e., failure mode (C) [12] [10]. By plotting the failure modes as functions of the steel plate
thickness, tst in Figure 9.19, it can be seen, that a more ductile failure mode, can not be obtained with
the used parameters. Failure mode (B) is not fully included on the plot, as it yields much higher values,
except for smaller values of tst. Failure mode (A) is governing for steel thicknesses above 2 mm.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
99
==PAGE:107==
CHAPTER 9. VERTICAL SHEAR CONNECTION
Figure 9.19: Failure modes [kN] vs steel plate thickness [mm]
A steel plate thickness of tst = 10 mm is adopted. A smaller value could also be sufficient.
9.5.3
Verification of Connection
The resulting design of the shear connection can be seen in Figure 9.20. The height of the steel plate
should be at least 20 Г— 50 mm = 1 m, as determined in section 9.2, to account for the eccentricity
moment, which is easy to comply with. Preferably, it should be much longer, 5 вҲ’10 m. The recessed
groove is extended 10 mm to each side beyond the width of the steel plate, to avoid contact stresses from
the plate to the CLT due to rotation of the steel plate.
Figure 9.20: Shear connection design: elevation (left) and horizontal cross-section (right), measures in
[mm]
The capacity of the connection shall be verified through investigating the resistance to the following
failure modes, where the lowest value dictates the capacity of the connection:
вҖў Shear failure of dowels
вҖў Embedment failure of steel plates
вҖў Shear failure of steel plates

Shear Failure of Dowels
The shear failure of the dowel is governed by the previously investigated failure modes. The investigation
in subsection 9.5.1 is used as a basis to determine the placement of the steel plate, so that the outer
member thickness is t1 = 45 mm. With the characteristic yield moment, My.k = 191 Nm, provided by
Rothoblaas, and the calculated embedment strength, fh,1,k = 11.8 MPa, the governing failure mode is
(A). The design shear capacity of a single dowel is found to be Rv,Rd = 28.7 kN.
Embedment Failure of Steel Plates
In prEN 1995-1-1 [10], an embedment strength of 600 MPa is assumed, which is stated to be conservative.
The approach in DS/EN 1993-1-1 is used to verify this. By using S235 steel plates with characteristic
ultimate tensile strength, fuk = 355 MPa, the design embedment capacity for two steel plates is found to
be 184 kN. This leads to an estimated characteristic embedment strength of 777 MPa, which verifies the
conservatism of the assumption used in the shear verification of the dowel.
Shear Failure of Steel Plates
The steel plates are verified for plate shear buckling in acc. with DS/EN 1993-1-5. The method is not
precisely applicable for this scenario, and the steel plates are simplifiedly considered as simply supported
plates with a length equal to the distance between the fasteners, 350 mm. The calculation yields a design
critical buckling stress of 113 MPa. The stress in the steel plates is estimated manually, as the design
shear flow distributed between the plates.
Alternatively, the steel plates can be verified for shear through the net cross-section in acc. with DS/EN
1993-1-1 [33] and DS/EN 1993-1-1 DK NA [20]. The net cross-section is assumed to be in the line
between two vertical fasteners of length 332 mm, yielding a shear capacity for two plates of 819 kN.
It can be concluded, that the steel plates are not very critical.
Net Shear Failure of CLT
The reduced cross section of the CLT has to withstand the shear stresses. The nodes between the layers
are further reduced due to the possibility of cracks, and the effective net cross-section is marked on
Figure 9.23. The nodes closest to the steel plates are discarded, since their contribution to the effective
thickness will be small.
Figure 9.23: Net reduced cross section of CLT
The shear stresses are larger further away from the center of the wall. The shear flow is therefore inves-
tigated 85 mm to both sides of the center, as can be seen in Figure 9.24, which corresponds to the width
of the recessed hole of the steel plate.

9.5.4
Summary
Asummary of the conducted verifications can be seen in Table 9.8. The steel plates yield very low degrees
of utilization and will most likely not be the cause of failure. As the vertical fastener distance was based
on the capacity of the dowel, the utilization for this is high, and it will with high probability be the cause
of failure. If predictions are correct, the connection will fail by embedment failure of the dowel in the
CLT.

9.5.5
Design Optimization
Ductility
In reality, the load will be distributed to the remaining dowels, and the joint probably not fail until even
higher loads to the wall are reached. It should therefore not be extremely critical to not have obtained the
most ductile failure mode.
Obtaining a more ductile failure mode would still be an optimization. This can be achieved by, e.g.,
decreasing the diameter of the dowels. These were determined without consideration of the ductility of
the joint. The failure modes are similarly plotted in Figure 9.25 with M12 STA dowels, where an outer
member thickness of t1 = 42 mm is iteratively determined. With these parameters, failure mode (C)
can be obtained for tst = 2-20 mm, where a thickness of 10 mm would be good. By using M12 dowels,
consequently, the capacity is smaller, and the number of fasteners needed would have to be increased
correspondingly.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
103
==PAGE:111==
CHAPTER 9. VERTICAL SHEAR CONNECTION
Figure 9.25: Failure modes [kN] vs steel plate thickness [mm] with M12 STA dowels
Number of Fasteners
The stiffness of the connection was less than what was used to determine the design load. Consequently,
there is room for optimization with respect to lowering the design load on the connection. By doing so,
the distance between the fasteners, and thereby the number of fasteners, can be reduced. Further, the
number of fasteners can be reduced in the upper floors, since the shear flow is less there.
The 5-story wall is considered. The FE-model is modified by changing the vertical shear stiffness to
ks = 69 kN
mm/m, and the resulting shear flow can be seen in Figure 9.26. Neglecting the local peak at
the bottom, the largest value of 74 kN
m is adapted. Above the connection at 13 m height, the shear flow of
52 kN
m is adapted.
Figure 9.26: Shear flow [kN/m] (LC4), with reduced stiffness
With the reduced shear flow, the distance between the fasteners can be reduced accordingly. The number
of fasteners before and after optimization can be seen in Table 9.9. The number of fasteners is reduced
by 18 per 5-story wall, corresponding to a 15% reduction.

By an FE-model of the wall with the corresponding reduced stiffnesses, the thresholds of stresses, reac-
tions and deformations were found not to be exceeded for the optimized design.
There is room for further optimization by repeating the iterative process, or by subdividing the fastener
distances even more. But at some point, it will require more detailed analyses due to varying stiffnesses,
and there are also advantages to keeping the design simple. For each iteration, the reduction in number
of fasteners will also be smaller.
9.5.6
Reflection
Placement of steel plates and fasteners in relation to board orientation and layer faces have not been in-
vestigated, which could affect capacity and stiffness. Placement between two layers might cause splitting
of the wood, and there is no standardized method of calculating this manually. Acc. to CLT Handbook,
US Edition [34], a fastener installed in the gaps between two boards, can cause the strength and stiffness
to be significantly smaller than estimated by the designer, since gaps as big as 6 mm can be found between
the edges of the boards. This aspect could be incorporated in the fastener distance, by accounting for the
board widths.
Another thing to investigate further, could be the length of the steel plate. From a static point of view,
it should be as long as possible. It would therefore be relevant to investigate the max. length for han-
dling/buildability. A more exact value for the width of the recessed groove could also be investigated
further.
Using different methods to calculate embedment strengths, different capacities can be obtained. There
are always insecurities in calculations, especially regarding material parameters and stiffnesses, why
the actual failure mode, capacity of the connection, and load in the connection might deviate from the
predictions.
Detailed calculations regarding this section can be seen in Appendix A.15.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

10
Experimental Testing of Shear Connection
The connection that was concluded in chapter 9 with dowels and two recessed steel plates, is experimen-
tally tested in this section, which is setup as a test report. The reporting of the test is generally following
the requirements in DS/ISO 6891.
10.1
General Introduction
The number of experimental investigations of laterally loaded dowel-type fasteners in CLT is limited. The
purpose of this test is to experimentally measure the capacity and stiffness of a shear connection, purposed
to vertically connect two CLT wall-panels. The two characteristics are analytically determined in acc.
with Eurocodes, which will serve as basis for evaluation and comparison of results. The standardized
methods for analytically determining the stiffness and the capacity of shear connections in CLT do not
account for layer build-up of the CLT and are thereby simplified. It shall be reviewed, if the layer build-up
influences these. Therefore, it is also difficult to obtain precise results, why deviations are expected.
Furthermore, the mounting of the connection is a great exercise for reviewing the buildability.
The main required equipment for a single test is listed below, where item 1, 2 and 3 constitute a single
test specimen and are replaced for each test.
1. CLT-element
Г—1
2. Steel dowel
Г—1 or 2
3. Steel plates
Г—2
4. Threaded steel rods
Г—2
5. Steel profile
Г—2
6. Computer with loading program
Г—1
7. Instron axial compression machine
Г—1
8. Flat load transfer head
Г—1
9. Metal plate for load distribution
Г—1
10.2
Test Setup and Performance
Adrawing of the test configuration can be seen in Figure 10.1, where relevant information and dimensions
are provided. The Instron-machine is not shown on the drawing. The external load, F, should be evenly
distributed between the two steel plates. The threaded rods and the steel profile hold the specimen in
place. The load cell is placed directly above the center of the dowels, to only load them in shear. The
steel plates are placed 5 mm from the bottom of the grooves in the CLT, to avoid unintentional contact
stresses. The holes in the steel are pre-drilled with a diameter of 16.5 mm. The holes in the CLT are
pre-drilled with a diameter of 16 mm. The bottom dowels were removed after the first test.

machine (A) is the machine surrounding the test specimen (B). The specimen is held in place by the steel
profile (C), which is mounted to the bottom by two threaded rods (D). The load cell (E) has an attached
load transfer head (F), and further is placed a metal plate (G) to distribute the load to the steel plates in
the specimen. The dark-brown burning marks at the edges of the CLT are due to the elements getting
close to the machinery of the cutting machine. They only affect the surface and should not interfere with
the test results.

knock out the bottom dowel on all the specimens, so that the ultimate load could be reached. Specimen
T2 was reused for the test with one dowel.

10.3.1
Estimated Maximum Load
The ultimate characteristic shear capacity of the dowels is calculated based on the mean of the measured
densities of the CLT. The estimated maximum load in eq. (10.1), is the characteristic shear capacity of the
dowels, calculated according to the theory presented in prEN 1995-1-1, by using the embedment strength
in Equation 3.50 presented by Petersen [6] of fh,k = 17.1 MPa with the mean value of the measured
densities. The used material parameters can be seen in section 10.4.
Fest = nf В· Rv,k
(10.1)
Deviations are to be expected, since there have not been done many similar tests with CLT. The properties,
hereunder the directions of the individual layers, are thereby not accounted for. To ensure that the steel
plates do not fail in buckling, they are calculated as columns in acc. with DS/EN 1993-1-1, and are
estimated to have a total column buckling capacity of 383 kN. As the machine can only reach 100 kN,
this should not be the cause of failure.

10.5
Test Execution
10.5.1
Steps
The steps for conducting each test are listed below. Prior to the steps, it is important to make sure, that
the testing machine is calibrated.
1. Measure dimensions of CLT-element, dowels and steel plates.
2. Predrill holes in the steel plates.
3. Predrill holes in the CLT-element.
4. Make recessed grooves in CLT-element.
5. Place steel plates in recessed grooves in CLT-element, so the holes align, using a hammer.
6. Knock dowel through the holes using a hammer.
7. Place test specimen in Instron-machine and mount the specimen with the available rods and steel
profile.
8. Load specimen acc. to subsection 10.5.2.
9. Repeat the preceding steps for each test.
10.5.2
Loading Procedure
The specimens are loaded with a constant displacement rate of 3 mm
min until failure, force capacity of the
Instron-machine, or a displacement of 15 mm is reached.
The original plan was to follow the loading procedure in DS/ISO 6891, where the elastic slip in the joints
is evaluated, but due to technical issues with the loading program, the described procedure was used.
10.6
Analysis
The program outputs of the software are a range of values of measured force, F in [kN], applied dis-
placement, u in [mm], and time t in [s]. It also outputs a slope, corresponding to a slip modulus. As the
loading procedure in DS/EN 26891 was not used, the formulas are also not directly applicable for the
data. The results will therefore not be directly comparable with similar tests. Strains in the steel and in
the CLT beyond the joint are assumed to be marginal.
10.6.1
Stiffness
It is unknown, how the program calculates the outputted slip modulus. To compare, the tangential slip
modulus at 40% of the max. load is calculated in acc. with DS/EN 26891, but by replacing Fest with

10.6.2
Failure
Based on the methods in prEN 1995-1-1 for dowel-type connections with multiple shear planes, the
expected failure mode is either (A): embedment failure of the CLT members, or (F): embedment failure
in the inner CLT-member and one plastic hinge in the dowel in each side. These two failure modes show
the lowest resistance, and the values per dowel can be seen in eq. (10.8).

10.7
Results
The obtained load-displacement diagram for specimen T2 with two dowels can be seen in Figure 10.7. It
shows a high stiffness, displacing only about 0.25 mm per 10 kN after the joint settlement. At 100 kN, the
displacement is only just above 3 mm. Visually, the slope seems to be a close fit. As the curve after the
joint settlement is close to linearity, the displacements are probably in the elastic range, and the specimen
not damaged. Furthermore, no visual damage was detected. Therefore, the bottom dowels were removed,
and the following tests are with one dowel only.

To compare the tests, it is convenient to plot the diagrams over each other, as done in Figure 10.11. The

specimens. This could be due to differences in installation accuracy. The connection shows ductility, as
there is a large ratio between the displacement at the ultimate load and at the yield load.
Test T2 with two dowels shows significantly higher stiffness, as would be expected. Correspondingly,
the joint settlement is significantly lower. It would have been interesting to see, how the specimen would
behave for larger loads.

varies solely with the density between the specimens (in analytical calculations). This does however not

The initial stiffnesses provided by the program are highly similar to the manually calculated values (вүҲ2%

The ultimate stiffnesses are вүҲ90% of Ku, but the values are also quite close to ks. As the linear-elastic
range is quite large, it would perhaps be more fitting to read ku at a higher load value.
10.7.1
Failure Mode
All three tests failed by embedment failure in the CLT, i.e, failure mode (A).
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
116
==PAGE:124==
CHAPTER 10. EXPERIMENTAL TESTING OF SHEAR CONNECTION
Specimen T2 was cut open, to investigate the failure mode in closer detail; see Figure 10.12. It is clear,
that the CLT failed due to the embedment stresses. It could then also be verified, that the steel plates
and dowel suffered no significant damage. The deformations in the CLT differs between the parallel-
to-load and crossing layers. The two crossing layers underwent elastic deformations, as some of their
deformation has reversed after unloading. Tuhkanen [16] calls this a вҖқlocking effectвҖқ in his testing of the
embedment strength of dowels in CLT, and states that it is more strongly pronounced for thicker layers,
since it happens due to the adjacent surfaces preventing splitting of individual layers.
(a) Without steel
(b) With steel
Figure 10.12: Cross-section of specimen T2 after failure
The crossing layers have also cracked in their grain direction, as can be seen in Figure 10.13, to which
the following text refers to. (A) shows the crossing layer in comparison to a parallel-to-load layer (B),
where the cracking is clearly visible. In (C) it can be seen, that the cracking happens to the edge of the
CLT-element. These cracks could already be seen on the outside of the specimens during testing (D), and
they were the first visual signs of failure for all three tests. Tuhkanen [16] observed similarly, and defines
it as a вҖқrope effectвҖқ, where tension in the crossing layers under the dowel causes shear between the fibers
in the same layer.
Figure 10.13: Cracks in crossing layers
10.7.2
Review of the Estimated Capacity
Using the embedment strength proposed in prEN 1995-1-1 [10] (still using the measured mean density),
the shear capacity per dowel becomes Rv,k = 74.7 kN, which is closer to the measured capacity, but not
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
117
==PAGE:125==
CHAPTER 10. EXPERIMENTAL TESTING OF SHEAR CONNECTION
sufficiently close. This embedment strength of fh,k = 32.5 MPa is 90% larger than by using the formula
by Petersen [6]. The calculated resistance for the obtained failure mode (A) is then Rv,k,A = 92 kN,
indicating that the yield moment of the dowels is significantly higher.
Using also the characteristic yield moment proposed in prEN 1995-1-1, the shear capacity per dowel
becomes Rv,k = 86.3 kN, which is much closer. This characteristic yield moment of My,k = 283 Nm is
48% larger than by using the value provided by the manufacturer.
Using a different embedment strength of the steel plates does not change the capacity much, except if it
is lowered significantly.
Collectively, the material parameters of the CLT and the dowels have probably been underestimated.
10.8
Discussion and Conclusions
The mounting of the connection was relatively free of difficulties. The insertion of the steel plates required
hammering and precision, and the additional depth of the recessed groove in the CLT made it a little
difficult to place precisely. It could therefore be difficult to mount a longer steel plate. Perhaps, the
recessed groove should be of the same depth as the steel plate. The dowels were easy to knock in; the
second one easier than the first. They were also easy to knock out.
The obtained failure mode was within the predictions for all tests, but the magnitude of the ultimate
load was far beyond. As mentioned, there are many insecurities in the calculations of connections in
CLT; especially regarding the embedment strength. The yield moment of the dowels has probably been
larger than specified by the manufacturer, as no plastic hinges were observed. By using the formulas in
prEN 1995-1-1 with the measured mean density, a sufficiently accurate capacity could be reached; this
is however still based on characteristic values of the steel.
The crossing layers could not have contributed much to the bearing capacity, due to the cracks and elastic
deformations. There is no known way to quantify this, and it would be relevant to test the same connection
with a larger share of crossing layers.
The initial stiffness was overestimated with commonly used formulas in DS/EN 1995-1-1 and prEN 1995-
1-1. The test with two dowels were especially underestimated, and only slightly stiffer than the tests with
one dowel. The slip modulus for the ultimate limit state is closer to the predicted value, but there are
uncertainties regarding the used data processing method.
It must be emphasized, that the sample size of the testing was very small. More tests could yield different
results.
More graphs and pictures can be seen in Appendix A.16.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
118
==PAGE:126==
11
Conclusions and Reflection
Conclusions and discussions have been presented in previous chapters and sections. Selected main points
are extracted to this chapter along with some final words.
11.1
Conclusions
Some of the main conclusions are provided in the following.
вҖў The proposed architectural floor plan, that was designed by Waugh-Thistleton Architects, can be
optimized in relation to building stability by small changes to the core layout.
вҖў The core is sufficient to stabilize the building for up to 5 stories, by the use of balloon framing with
CLT-walls.
вҖў Wind load has shown to be the dominant horizontal load type in both building directions. Seis-
mic load might be dominant in the longitudinal direction, if the buildingвҖҷs length to width ratio is
increased.
вҖў The anchor reaction force has yielded to be a good indicator for determining which CLT-wall will
be critical, for walls with similar properties.
вҖў The largest degrees of utilization for the CLT-walls has been in-plane tensile and shear stresses and
horizontal deflection, and the load combinations with dominating wind load has shown to be the
most critical.
вҖў The large floor diaphragm cantilevers between the gables and the core causes larger horizontal
loads on the outermost walls in the core. For higher buildings, additional stabilizing elements is
therefore needed in these areas, if the CLT-core is to be used as a stabilizing element. Alternatively,
a concrete core can be implemented.
вҖў For stabilizing balloon-frame CLT-walls, the stiffnesses in connections are of high importance, with
regards to how the wall behaves, and how the load is distributed.
вҖў Using dowels and internal steel plates has shown to be a promising joint type for connecting wall
panels vertically with high stiffness and capacity.
11.2
Reflection
Some final thoughts, ideas, and subjects for further analyses are presented in this section.
вҖў The stiffnesses have a large influence on how the load is distributed. It can be difficult to calculate
true stiffnesses, why the designers must be considerate when distributing the load. Using the trib-
utary area method might have been a conservative approach, and less loads on the outermost walls
might be a more realistic scenario.
Thinner CLT-walls would most probably be sufficient for 3-4 stories, but this should be the subject
for a further analysis.
вҖў Fire safety and dynamic loads have not been considered, and should be subjects for further analyses;
especially for the vertical shear connection.
вҖў The vertical shear connection could be designed to be more ductile by using weaker dowels. The
internal steel plates could be thinner without significant changes to the capacity and failure mode.
As the anchor connection most probably will fail before the vertical shear connection, the stiffness
is therefore a more dominant parameter, than the strength.
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
119
==PAGE:127==
CHAPTER 11. CONCLUSIONS AND REFLECTION
вҖў There have not been distinguished between stiffnesses in ULS and SLS in the analytical investiga-
tions of the vertical shear connection. Should a higher stiffness be needed, the fastener distances
can simply be reduced.
вҖў A high vertical panel interface stiffness have been used in the FE-calculations of the CLT-walls,
when analyzing the stabilizing systems. The value is achievable but is too large in comparison to
what was found in later analyses. The base shear stiffness might have been too low in the FE-
calculations, but the effects on the results should be marginal.
Furthermore, some of the FE-analyses have been based on peak stresses, which in retrospect might
have been a conservative choice.
вҖў Based on the conducted experiments, the common formulas for the slip modulus are very general,
and does not predict the actual stiffness very well. The cause of the underestimation in SLS is
unknown but could be due to the use of CLT and/or to the larger amount of shear planes.
вҖў The analyses in this project has been based on a variety of assumptions; mainly regarding loads.
Using different assumptions might yield different results.

Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

==PAGE:131==
Surface Dead Loads
The characteristic surface dead load of floors, facades and roof is estimated in the following. In reality there 
will be different build-ups for different sections of the surfaces, but to simplify, average loads are estimated. A 
minimum and a maximum load is determined for each surface, since both a large and small vertical load can be 
unfavorable for the stabilizing elements.
Weights of thin lightweight membranes like vapor barriers and air tightness membranes are neglected.

Weight of floor types
In the following, the weight of different floor build-ups are estimated. The weight of the CLT is excluded, and 
will be added afterwards.
Floor Type 1-4 are common types given as examples in the Build-in-Wood guide.
Floor Type 5-8 are common types given as examples in The CLT Handbook by Swedish Wood.

To estimate realistic values, the outliers, being Floor Type 2, 3, 5, and 8, are disregarded. Floor Type 1 is then 
the heaviest realistic build-up, and will be used as the starting point for the largest dead load. Floor type 2 is 
then the lightest realistic build-up, and will be used as the starting point for the largest dead load.

Wind Load
The wind load is calculated in accordance with DS/EN 1991-1-4 DK NA:2015 in the following. The wind load 
is divided into different pressure zones, which depend on the main dimensions of the building. To simplify, the 
wind load on the roof is "smeared" out on the whole roof surface by using the average wind load for the four 
scenarios: wind load on faГ§ade and gable with suction and compression.

Extension of zones

respect to the wind direction. The extensions of the zones are therefore calculated for each building height for 
both wind directions.

APPENDIX A.3. WIND LOAD
Matlab code Main.m that calculates wind load in both directions of the building:
1
% ======================= Input ======================= %
2
nst=8;
% Number of storys
3
hst=4;
% Story height [m]
4
ED=0.5;
% Extra dimension [m]
5
W=54+ED;
% Building width [m]
6
D=27+ED;
% Building depth [m]
7
H=hst*nst+ED;
% Building Height [m]
8
terkat=2;
% Terrain category 0/1/2/3/4
9
10
% ======================= Wind Load ======================= %
11
% Characteristic wind loads, surface load [N/m2]
12
% at top and at z=d for z>d
13
wk_0=zeros(6,2);
14
wk_90=wk_0;
15
for i=3:8
16
wk_0(i-2,:)=Wind_Load(i*hst+ED,D,terkat);
% Wind at facade
17
wk_90(i-2,:)=Wind_Load(i*hst+ED,W,terkat);
% Wind at gable
18
end
19
20
% Charactaristic wind loads, line load [N/m]
21
wk_0_l=zeros(nst,nst);
22
wk_90_l=wk_0_l;
23
for i=3:8
24
wk_0_l(1:i,i) = Wind_Line_Load(i,hst,wk_0,D,ED);
25
wk_90_l(1:i,i) = Wind_Line_Load(i,hst,wk_90,W,ED);
26
end
27
28
% Characteristic wind loads, equivalent point load [N]
29
Wk_0=wk_0_l*W;
30
Wk_90=wk_90_l*D;
Matlab code Wind_ Load.m that Main.m uses to calculate the wind load:
1
function [wk] = Wind_Load(H,D,terkat)
2
3
% D
Building dimension parallel to wind [m]
4
% terkat
Terrain category (0, 1, 2, 3, 4)
5
z=H;
% Reference height [m]
6
7
% ======================= Peak velocity pressure ======================= %
8
z0=[0.003, 0.01, 0.05, 0.3, 1]';
9
z0=z0(terkat+1);
% z0 [m]
10
kr=0.19*(z0/0.05)^0.07;
% [-]
11
rho=1.25;
% Air density during storm [kg/m3]
12
vb=24;
% Basis wind speed [m/s]
13
14
% Peak velocity pressure [N/m2]
15
qp_max=(1+7/log(z/z0))/2*rho*(vb*kr*log(z/z0))^2;
16
qp_min=(1+7/log(min(z,D)/z0))/2*rho*(vb*kr*log(min(z,D)/z0))^2;
17
18
% ======================= Mean wind ======================= %
19
zmin=[1, 1, 2, 5, 10]';
20
zmin=zmin(terkat+1);
% zmin [m]
21
zmax=200;
% [m]
22
cr=max(log(z/z0),log(zmin/z0))*kr;
% Roughness factor [-]
23
co=1;
% Orography factor [-]
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures
140
==PAGE:148==
APPENDIX A.3. WIND LOAD
24
vm=cr*co*vb;
% Mean wind [m/s]
25
26
% ======================= Shape factor ======================= %
27
h=z;
% Building height [m]
28
d=D;
% Building dimension parallel to wind [m]
29
30
% Shape factors for zone D and zone C, and correlation factor [-]
31
if h/d > 5
32
"building is too high"
33
rho_c=1;
34
end
35
if h/d
вүӨ5 && h/d > 1
36
cD=0.8;
37
cE=interp1([1,5],[0.5,0.7],h/d);
38
rho_c=interp1([1,5],[0.85,1],h/d);
39
end
40
if h/d
вүӨ1 && h/d > 0.25
41
cD=interp1([0.25,1],[0.7,0.8],h/d);
42
cE=interp1([0.25,1],[0.3,0.5],h/d);
43
rho_c=0.85;
44
end
45
if
h/d
вүӨ0.25
46
cD=0.7;
47
cE=0.3;
48
rho_c=0.85;
49
end
50
c=(cD+cE)*rho_c;
% Corresponding total shape factor [-]
51
wk_max=qp_max*c;
% Characteristic wind load at top [N/m2]
52
wk_min=qp_min*c;
% Characteristic wind load at z=d for z>d [N/m2]
53
wk=[wk_min,wk_max];
The wind load is distributed into the floor diaphragms with the code/function Wind_Line_Load.m:
1
function [wk_line] = Wind_Line_Load(nst,hst,wk,d,ED)
2
3
% Wind load in first floor diaphragm [N/m]
4
wk=wk(nst-2,:);
5
wk_line=zeros(nst,1);
6
wk_line(1)=wk(1)*hst;
7
% If the building width is less than 1.5 times the floor height , the wind
8
% load is not constant in the first floor diaphragm (not relevant for
9
% project).
10
11
% Wind load in floor diaphragms [N/m]
12
for st=2:(nst-1)
13
if st*hstвүӨ(d-0.5*hst)
14
wk_line(st)=wk(1)*hst;
15
end
16
if st*hst<d && st*hst>(d-hst/2)
17
wk_line(st)=wk(1)*(hst/2+(d-hst*st)) + wk(2)*(hst/2-(d-hst*st));
18
end
19

Seismic Load
The seismic loading is calculated according to DS/EN 1998-1 DK NA:2020 Annex D as a percentage of the 
vertical load by a factor. The factor differs for different building geometries, and is therefore calculated for 
building heights of 3-8 stories.
The seismic load is to be determined by the formula:

Dead load of columns
The column sizes are expected to vary along the building height, and this affects the seismic load. All 
sizes are assumed to be equal on each floor.

In the following, the distribution of horizontal reactions in the shear walls from horizontal load on the facade is calculated using the "rigid 
diaphragm method". The rigid diaphragm method assumes infinitely rigid floor slabs (diaphragms), and considers the shear walls as beams 
clamped in the foundation.

Load Distribution using FEM
Assumed connections of panel-to-panel of slabs and panel-to-beam [drawing from Build-in-Wood]
Mean density of CLT

Kser =
Kser,i/s

24.95 kN/mm/m
The load distribution is investigated with a FEM-model of the floor diaphragms and walls. 
The floor diaphragms are modelled as 210mm elements with 7x30mm layers of C24 with 5 
layers spanning vertically and 2 spanning horizontally. The walls are modelled as 210mm 
elements with 7x30mm layers of C24 with 5 layers spanning vertically and 2 spanning 
horizontally.
Different stiffness between the panels are modelled. The walls are supported without spring stiffnesses, 
but with a spring stiffness of 85 kN/mm/m between its panels. This is a value, that is determined later on 
in the project. The slabs are loaded with a unit load (100kN/54m/2=0.926kN/m), so that the horizontal 
reaction resultants can represent the percentages of the load they take.
Load Distribution

Reaction force resultants in walls with different stiffnesses for the wall base
Load distribution for different slab stiffnesses
The stiffness of the panel connection has an influence on the load distribution, however, it is not major 
for the considered establishment. The in-plane stiffnessses of the element are most probably more 
dominant than the spring stiffnesses, why the spring stiffness have little influence on the load 
distribution. The load distribution for the 25 kN/mm/m spring is adapted.
Now, the wall will be modelled with the adapted stiffness between the panels of 25 kN/mm/m, and the 
stiffness of the shear connection at the bottom of the wall is varied. This variation should also represent 
any other deviations in stiffnesses from, e.g, the anchor, lift and the panel interface. In the fully rigid 
case, the wall is removed and just modeled as a simple rigid support.

Load distribution with different element thicknesses
Load distribution for different wall stiffnesses
Also the stiffness of the wall influences the load distribution. The influence is small. 
It will now be investigated, how the different in-plane stiffnesses influences the distribution. Different 
wall and floor element thicknesses are therefore investigated. The 150mm thickness represents 5 layers of 
30mm, and the 280mm thickness represesents 7 layers of 40mm. All else is the same.

Load distibution with 150mm walls and 280mm floors and no springs
Load distribution for different wall and slab thicknesses
Comparing with the same wall thicknesses with springs, the load distribution does not change 
significantly. 
It is concluded, that the less stiff the walls are compared to the floors, the more evenly the load will be 
distributed. The less stiff the slabs are, the less evenly the load will be distributed.
The actual distribution of the load will be based on some relationship between the stiffnesses.
The larger the stiffness of the wall compared to the floor, the larger deviance in the distribution. The 
stiffer the floors compared to the walls, the more evenly the load is distributed.
It is investigated, how the load distribution will differ, if all springs are hinged instead; see the figure 
below.

FEM
Interval values*
Amount of load taken by walls for different distribution methods
Different manually calculated load distributions have been investigated. Now they are summarized, and 
d
Rigid diaphragm method
Tributary area method
Elastic
The rigid diagram method yields an equal distribution of the load between the walls. The plastic distribution yields a 
greater amount of load taken by the walls, that has a larger horizontal load tributary area.
The rigid diagram method yields more load taken by the longer, stiffer walls. The elastic distribution yields a greater 
amount taken by the longer walls, and the largest amount of load is if the load is distributed by stiffness. The plastic 
Which load distribution method is critical depends on what and which wall is being investigated, and can therefore not 
be concluded. The different load distribution reactions in the walls, can be used as minimum and maximum values. 
Common for most of the methods is, that the walls with the largest tributary areas take the most loads.

Determination of Critical Wall
In the following, it is estimated which horizontal load type (wind load or seismic load) is critical, and which 
shear wall will be critical.
Critical horizontal load type
The determination of the critical load type is based on the base shear force, and not the individual shear force 
in every floor. Different horizontal force types can be critical for the same wall in different levels. The 
following is therefore an estimation, but gives a good idea of which forces to examine further.

Overall critical wall
To determine which walls to investigate further, all walls are considered. Design anchor forces are summed 
up below for each wall and each number of stories.
The anchor forces in wall 1y and 5y seem to be largest, whereof wall 5y have slightly larger values than wall 
5y. Wall 5y will therefore most probably be the most critical shear wall for all building heights.
Discussion
Which wall(s) to be examined further has been investigated. From the considerations, wall 5y is critical, and 
should be investigated for anchor forces, stresses and deformations. It has been an estimate, and several 
parameters has not been considered.
It is also seen, that the same wall is critical for all considered no. of stories.

Walls in Initial Floor Plan
The anchor force is estimated using the FE-software FEM-Design and hand calculation, and is used to
determine the max. height of the building without the use of additional stabilizing elements.
Calculation of anchor force
Material
The panel used for this preliminary analysis is a SГ¶dra CLT 210-L7s2 panel, which is a 7-layer panel with a 
thickness of 210mm and a layer thickness of 30mm, with the outermost two layers spanning in the same
direction (vertically).
Furthermore, the model has the following properties:


No glue at narrow sides (no edge glue)
Shear coupling between layers is considered
The panel is predefined in FEM-Design, but the density has been modified to match the inferior dead load in 
this project 
вү”
ПҒCLT.inf
350 вҖ•вҖ•
kg
m
3
Geometry
The walls are supported in the bottom only. They are supported by the anchor connection in tension with 
geometry and stiffness acc. to Dimmock, B.S., in compression by contact pressure, and in shear in the 
compression zone. It will thereby only be supported laterally where there is compression.

Influence of mesh
To investigate the influence of a finer mesh and an element type with more nodes, a model of the building 
with five stories is made with a finer mesh, to re-calculate the anchor force. The mesh is refined by average 
element size, and refined even further in the bottom of the wall.

Base shear connection
The base shear connection should withstand the design shear force. "Titan F" 
is an angle bracket available from Rothoblaas, that is designed for the 
purpose of transferring shear forces from a CLT-wall to a perpendicular 
surface in wood or concrete. A TCF200 bracket with Гҳ5x50mm LBS screws 
in all bracket holes (30) in the wood mounted to uncracked concrete with 
2xM12 8.8 130mm anchors is considered.

Vertical shear stiffness
A quick investigation is done to review, if the vertical panel interface could be sufficiently rigid to decrease the 
anchor force beneath the threshold for a 6 story wall. The 5- and 6-story wall is therefore modified with a rigid 
vertical panel interface.
As seen on the figure to the left, the anchor force is reduced a bit, but not 
sufficiently. It is also noted, that a rigid connection is theoretical.

Conclusion
With the presumed conditions regarding stiffnesses, loads, geometry, etc., the layout of shear walls in the 
transverse building direction is not sufficient for the building with more than 5 stories, due to the anchor force 
exceeding the assumed capacity. It might be possible with optimization. Stresses and deformations has yet to 
be investigated.

The loads can be seen below. The self weight of the wall is added by the program.
The effects on forces and stresses from skew loading are also investigated. The following load cases are 
therefore also defined. The load scenarios are considered highly unlikely, but the effects are still relevant to 
investigate.
Two scenarios are investigated for the load combination with dominating wind load, inferior dead load and 
skew live load. The live load is still being reduced by 
in the load combination.
Оұn
The scenario with skew load on one side could affect the wall stresses and anchor force. The other scenario 
could affect the shear flow, especially if the coupling effect is pronounced.

Compressive, tensile and buckling stresses are based on the net area. Negative values indicate compression. 
The largest stresses for each load duration are shown.
The critical load combinations are:
P-load: 
Dead load dom.
M-load: Live load dom.
S-load: 
Live load dom. + snow
I-load: 
Wind load dom. + live 
load one side (left), LC4 
(right)
The buckling stress is investigated 
0.5m from the side,  and halfway 
between the stories, since the wall 
considered restrained from buckling 
by the adjacent, perpendicular walls 
and the slabs.

Buckling failure
The buckling resistance is calculated using two different methods; the method provided by Eurocode, and the 
method used by FEM-Design which includes shear flexibility. Both methods results in a column reduction 
factor, 
, where the smallest value will reduce the capacity the most.

Out-of-plane bending failure
Out-of-plane bending from eccentric vertical loading is not included in the FEM-model, but is verified 
manually. The load combination with dominating wind load + live load is considered. The bending stress is 
calculated manually, while the simultaneously acting compressive stresses are calculated with a FE-model.
A typical connection for a ballon-frame 
wall is assumed, as on the figure to the 
right, using brackets. An out-of-plane 
moment will be induced, if the moment 
resultants from the slabs from each side of 
the wall are non-equal.
Assumed type of connection between slabs and walls
The analytical model of the vertical load is assumed similar to the method commonly used for concrete elements.
Analytical model of eccentric load
Corbel/bracket depth and 
thickness

Rigid body rocking
Two contributions to rigid body rocking are added. One from slip in the bottom anchor force, and one from slip 
in the anchor force at approximately 13m height.  It is assumed that the ratio between the stiffness and the 
anchor force is the same for both connections, such that the lift contribution is the same for both connections.

Additional CLT Walls and Increased Wall Thickness
As the anchor force capacity has been the limiting parameter for the number of stories with the same layout, an 
increased anchor force capacity is investigated.
As the stresses also will play a role, CLT-walls with different cross-sections is investigated.
The characteristic loads on the model can be seen in the figure below, as well as the mesh, using 9-node 
quadrilateral elements and 6-node triangular elements.
Characteristic loads on FEM-model
FEM-mesh
The thickness of the lamellae are increased from 
30 to 45mm, since more capacity is needed. All 
else in the model is the same as in previous 
analyses.

The relevant load combinations are LC4 (dominating wind load with min. vertical load) and LC3 (dominating 
wind load and max. vertical load) with skewed load cases. Both combinations are of "instantaneous" load 
duration.
LC4: Anchor force resultant [kN]
LC3: Anchor force resultant [kN]
LC3 results in a slightly larger anchor force.

force for 6 stories

Anchor connection
It is investigated, how many fasteners are needed for the anchor connection to be able to withstand the load.
The connection utilizes the increased capacity from 
inclining VGS screws with VGU washers from 
Rothoblaas.

Ultimate Limit State
In ultimate limit state, it has been established, that in-plane tension, compression and shear are governing 
failure modes. Out-of-plane bending and buckling are not investigated.
In-plane tension and compression
LC3 results in the largest stresses for all in-plane 
compression and tension stresses in the x-direction.

Conclusion
The stresses exceed their respective capacities for in-plane vertical tension and in-plane shear for Wall 1 and 
Wall 2. Wall 3 has sufficient capacity for the investigated stresses. 
Serviceability Limit State
The deflections are investigated for the three walls, based 
on the characteristic load combination with dead load, 
horizontal wind load, and wind load on one side of the 
roof.

==PAGE:219==
The deflection barely complies with the threshold. The model is quite coarse with respect to the assumed 
stiffnesses, why the deflection might be larger or smaller in a more detailed analysis.
Furthermore, the threshold is adjustable, and something that is ultimately determined for each project. The 
threshold might therefore be a bit looser, e.g., around 
.
=
вҖ•вҖ•
h
450
53.33 mm
Finally, stiffnesses used for the shear connections are conservative, and might be much stiffer, when 
investigated in detail.
Conclusion
CLT wall thicknesses of 7x30mm and 9x30mm are not sufficient with the initial core layout only as a
stabilizing system for a 6-story building. It should be possible to use 7x45mm CLT walls without changing the 
stabilizing system. This is a quite thick wall, and it might take up too much space in the core. If the deflection 
criteria is tight, it might not be possible with the investigated wall thickness.

Additional CLT Walls
Readapting the 7x30mm wall, stabilizing systems with additional stabilizing elements are investigated for 
buildings of 6-8 floors.
Alternative 1
The first thing to consider is two additional CLT shear walls in the y-direction: one in grid line 2 and one in 
grid line 9.
Alternative 1: additional CLT shear walls in grid line 2 and 9

G.L. = Grid Line
Additional walls in grid line 9 and 2 are now more critical for 8 stories.
OBS: For 6 and 7 stories, the analysis yields, that wall 4y is more critical. The difference in anchor force for the 
walls in consideration is small, and the same conclusion will most probably be drawn, whichever wall is 
considered, unless the utilization degrees are very close to 100%. The newly added wall is analyzed in the 
following.
It is noted, that the anchor force in the walls in the x-direction now actually are largest. But as it is a possibility 
to activate more walls in that direction, the newly added walls are investigated. The x-direction will be 
investigated later.

Conclusion

optimization, detailed investigation or looser deflection limit. Lateral deflections of the wall is the governing 
factor.

Alternative 3 - long gable walls
For the solution with additional stiffer/longer walls in the gable, the plastic load distribution is too 
conservative. To utilize, that the walls are longer, the load has to be distributed based on stiffness/length. 
The anchor force is therefore calculated for two different alternatives of walls in the gables, using a 
distribution based on the stiffness of the walls.
Firstly, the load distributions are calculated:
Load distributions in walls for the two alternatives
The additional gable shear walls appear unload the core walls equally much with the considered load
distribution.
With the load distributions, the anchor force reactions can be approximated (faГ§ade dead load is now also 
included):
Design anchor forces with 12m CLT shear walls in the gables

and Increased Wall Thickness

227
==PAGE:235==
Design anchor forces with 7.5m CLT shear walls in the gables
The additional gable shear walls have anchor forces in the same range of magnitude. Based on previous 
calculations, the manual anchor force estimation is close to the one calculated with FEM. Based on that 
observation, the anchor forces are within the threshold for both alternatives.
The 12m wall will be investigated further, since it will be more critical than the 7.5m wall. If the stabilizing 
system is sufficient with the 12m walls, it is most probably also sufficient with the 7.5m walls.
Only the 8-story wall is considered in the following.
Model
The vertical support is just elongated from the previous models, and the anchor 
support remains in the same place and magnitude. The shear support spans 
between the centers of the outermost wall panels, i.e., it is 9m long.
Mesh (fine elements, avg. 
element size=0.2x0.2m)

Walls in the Buildings x-direction
It will now be investigated how many stories the building can have before needing additional stabilizing 
initiatives in the buildings x-direction.
It has already been established, that wall 3x will be the most critical wall in the x-direction. This is mainly due to 
its shorter length. Wall 3x is therefore considered.

Stiffness of system and load distribution
Firstly, it has to be determined how much load the walls can take. An assumption is made, that the lateral load 
is distributed between the walls and the braces by their respective stiffnesses, where the load in the braces is 
distributed evenly between the braces, and the load in the walls is distributed evenly between the walls.
An iterative process has been conducted on how much load the walls can take, before the anchor force reaction 
exceeds the assumed capacity of 
; see the results below:
пјқ
TRd
1369 kN
Design anchor forces in core walls for 11% of the horizontal load in each wall
It was found, that the anchor force capacity was exceeded by 12% in wall 2y (wall 2y has the least amount of 
favorable vertical load) for 8 stories. The max. load for each wall is therefore defined as 11%.

==PAGE:255==
8-story CLT wall
Wall 2y yielded the largest anchor reaction from the hand calculation. The hand calculation is simplified, and 
does not account for the skew tributary area for the vertical load. Wall 4y yielded an anchor reaction in the 
same range of magnitude, and its tributary area for vertical load is more skewed than wall 2y. Wall 4y is 
therefore considered as the more critical wall for horizontal load, and is chosen as the representative of the 
walls.

Bracing in single bay for 7 stories
It is investigated if less bracing is needed for a 7 story building. The placement can be seen in the figure below. 
Stabilizing elements on plan view: bracing in one 6m bay in both gables
An iteration to determine the max. amount of force in the walls is made, leading to the anchor forces as seen 
below:
Design anchor forces in core walls for 15% of the horizontal load in each wall
It was found, that the anchor force capacity was exceeded by 16% for 7 stories. The max. load for each wall is 
therefore defined as 15% for 7 stories.

utilization is very low).
Discussion/conclusion
For 5-8 stories, the stabilizing elements on the initial floor plan are not sufficient. This can be solved by 
introducing portal bracing in wood in the gables. These have to be sufficiently stiff to take enough horizontal 
load, and this will most probably be the governing factor for the bracing dimensions, as the utilization factors 
are relatively low in both SLS and ULS.
It should be possible to use bracing in a single bay to stabilize the building in addition to the core walls, for 
6-7 stories. The ULS-verification yielded low utilization degrees for all elements. There should be plenty of 
space for the placing of the bolts in both the bracing, beams and columns.

APPENDIX A.10. BRACING IN WOOD OR STEEL
Stability of Higher Office Buildings Made of Wood Structures

Steel bracing
The alternative with bracing in the two 7.5m bays is investigated for steel 
bracing as an alternative to glulam bracing.
Cross-bracing and V-bracing
Firstly, the stiffness of two different bracing systems are evaluated, 
vertical V-bracing and cross-bracing. The bracings are modelled as truss 
elements, that can only take tension.
A horizontal load is modelled as for the 7.5m bay earlier on 0.8kN/m. The 
columns and beams are of the same dimensions, and the truss-elements 
are of Гҳ152.4x10mm steel.
As can be seen, the deflection is much larger for the V-bracing. This is 
due to the cross-bracing being more effective for truss-structures, which 
was not the case for the wood bracing, but is the case for the steel bracing.
Cross-bracing is adopted for steel.
Horizontal deflection of 
inverted V-bracing (left) and 
cross-bracing (right)
Stiffness and load distribution
The equivalent stiffness of the bracing shall now be determined. Iteratively, a steel 
bar of Гҳ100x15mm S235 has been determined for sufficient stiffness and strength.

Max. design axial forces, LC4 [kN]
The design axial forces are smaller than for the previously investigated steel bracing system for 8 stories, 
except for the tensile force in the column, and the dimension of the beams and columns are the same. The 
compression force in the column is much larger than the tensile force, and a verification is not needed. The 
only verification needed for the structural elements, is therefore the steel truss in tension.

Anchorage of column connection
The capacity of an anchor connection is verified, to ensure, that it is possible to connect the bracing system to 
the foundation. Only the connection in the wood part is verified, since that will be the critical and interesting 
part. With wood bracing, the anchor force is smaller, and thereby the verification is valid for that also.
Column anchor connection (wind cross and connection to foundation not shown)

Beam connection
The beam-part of the steel-bracing-to-beam connection is considered. It will in the following be checked, if a 
bolted connection can be made in the beam, such that the forces can be transferred between the brace element 
and the beam.

Firstly, the 3-story building is investigated. Thereafter the 4-story building, and so forth, until the max. no. of stories is 
reached. The verification is as follows:
1) Check turnover of walls without anchoring.
2) Check compression zone without anchoring. "A" means the compression zone is too small. "NOT OK!" means the 
compressive stress is too large.
3) Check tensile capacity of anchoring.
4) Check sliding
No anchoring or shear reinforcement is needed for the 3-story building walls. They have sufficient capacity to withstand the
